# Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_244 messages · 29 participants · 2000-03 → 2000-04_

**Topics:** [`Object-oriented COBOL`](../topics.md#oo)

---

### Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

- **From:** mel5133928@aol.com (MEl5133928)
- **Date:** 2000-03-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com>`

```
Group,

What are your views on the difficulty or non-difficulty in making the
transition from COBOL to say C++ or JAVA? The reason I ask is that I remember
seeing an article which quoted a "leading" consultancy :) stating that COBOL
programmers performed poorly in this regard.

Cheers,
Mark
```

#### ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

- **From:** petwir@saif.com (Pete Wirfs)
- **Date:** 2000-03-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38c02889.69848657@news.transport.com>`
- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com>`

```
As a 20-year COBOL programmer that was given a Java project 2 months
ago, I can say it is very difficult for me.  Writing Java code is
easy, but doing 20 or 30 really tiny programs to do something that
could have been done in a single COBOL program is difficult to
swallow.

It is starting to sink in for me that the 30 tiny Java programs are in
fact re-usable and will save the effort of re-coding them in future
applications.  But this does not mean the next application will be
coded any faster; any time saved is spent in programmer time necessary
to locate and study the interfaces to these modules in order to call
them correctly the next time.  I believe OO is superior software
architecture, but it does not cut down software development time. 

Most literature I have read on the subject suggests that it can take
old procedural programmers (like me) several months longer to be
trained and become productive than programmers that were brought up on
OO concepts.  I believe they are correct.

Pete W




On 03 Mar 2000 20:14:50 GMT, mel5133928@aol.com (MEl5133928) wrote:
```

```
I don't know, I'd welcome a chance to learn Java, of course I've worked in
languages other than Cobol before but I think a programmer that has
developed a good structured Cobol style and has at least some exposure to
other languages, even if not OO, has a decent chance of making the
transition. For that matter I'd love to get involved in an OO Cobol
project, if I could find one, and learn about OO Cobol.
```

```
Pete Wirfs wrote:
> 
> As a 20-year COBOL programmer that was given a Java project 2 months
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
I can understand how you arrived at your conclusions, but I don't think
they are correct. Without any doubt there is that learning curve - in
COBOL, much of the same syntax, but not quite the same baby. I'd bet if
you had started with a COBOL OO environment, you would have been
similarly confused, but once you saw the wood for the trees you would
have experienced satisfaction in moving forward with some confidence.

You mention C and Java, (the latter because of involvement in a 'Net
based application ?). I'm willing to bet your confusion had nothing to
do with what we call typical COBOL business logic, but rather
inter-facing to Windows APIs or the 'Net.

You've hit the right button on the question of reusable methods, and
realize that they can be re-used. If you feel that this involves
'research' as you indicate to re-use them, then I think you've missed
the point somewhere in your design. Go back (no not GOBACK) and take a
critical look at those reusables of yours - have you properly got them
organized into relevant classes, or for that matter a set of hieracial
classes, with inheritance ?

A factor to remember is that COBOL is a business based language - that's
not something that shifts easily to the 'new' languages.

My attempts, (in OO COBOL) suggest that planning the structure and
location, (which class they are in), of those reusables is paramount to
effectivly invoking reusable methods.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
G Moore wrote:
> 
> petwir@saif.com (Pete Wirfs) wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
The above is only partially true. But in truth you don't get it; there
is much more to OO than just passing parameters, in the sense that "What
are you passing the parameters to ?" - this class, or the one above it,
or the one above that etc... - and it can go on and on. 

It doesn't just have the simplicity of calling Program B from Program A 
- although A is 'supposedly' calling B, A in fact can actually 'by-pass'
B because of inheritance and call C, D, E, F etc.... I should stress
that another factor is that you are not calling a hard-coded reference
to a sub-program - with object reference A can call B or X which may
contain another set of methods, e.g :-

	set CurrentCallObject to MyClassB
	invoke CurrentCallObject "method-1" returning ls-result

	set CurrentCallObject to MyClassX
	invoke CurrentCallObject "method-1" returning ls-result

the results from the two "methods-1" being entirely different.

		Class D inherits from Base
			- contains method 1
			  contains method 2

		Class C - inherits from Class D
			  contains method 3
			  contains method 4

		Class B - inherits from Class C
			  contains method 5
			  contains method 6

A calls B for method 1 	- bypasses B and C
A calls B for method 2	- ditto
A calls B for method 3	- bypasses B
A calls B for method 4	- ditto
A calls B for method 5 	- goes to B
A calls B for method 6	- ditto

And it isn't the rigid sequence I've shown above - you can permutate
those invokes into any sequence you want, Methods 7, 3, 9, 1, 2, 53 or
whatever.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
G Moore <gvwmoore@spam.ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
>
> I would just think that the compiler makers would see that the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

NOW, I understand why I haven't been able to figure out your entire point
about COBOL drivers.  I don't believe in your "first rule of programming" at
all.  I am a strong believer in the "right tool for the right job" vision of
programming languages.  One of the goals of the current COBOL revision work
was to IMPROVE inter-language operability.  This was NOT intended to make
COBOL the language that could handle every programming job - it was intended
to make certain that for that part of the job that COBOL was best for, it
could be coded in COBOL - and still interface (well) with C, Java, Assembler,
or whatever. For IBMers, this was definitely a part of the LE (Language
Environment) philosophy - and I also think is common in ANSI/ISO "language
independent arithmetic" work.

The bottom-line is that getting COBOL drivers makes sense BUT ONLY if your
goal is to reach a single programming language environment.  That isn't
something that I am interested in - and don't think that any of the COBOL
vendors are looking to either.
```

```
G Moore wrote:
> 
> "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:
> 
> the single programming language environment *assures* that you don't
> need multi-skilled programmers to keep the computer working.

Thank goodness. I've now found something where you and I can agree. But
I'm afraid we are in the minority on this one as Bill has already
indicated, coupled with the fact our COBOL compiler vendors don't warm
to the idea.

It's a hard road to slog - but if the COBOL community took the attitude
that we are going to take all these bright new languages head-on, then I
truly believe COBOL could plant them all in a cemetery ! Sure there will
always be somebody introducing a new language, because of assumed
advantages.  OK say our standards people, "Good features, worth
considering - stay tuned it will be part of COBOL 2010 !".

Now back to OO - and can't help feeling you have your eyes really closed
on this one. Just let your eyelids flicker a little - take a closer look
at Ken Foskey's latest message. I think he says it best in describing
the drawing of shapes. I'm not saying a clever programmer can't attempt
the same in structured, but it is infinitely more flexible in OO.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
James J. Gavan wrote:
>
>It's a hard road to slog - but if the COBOL community took the attitude
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>considering - stay tuned it will be part of COBOL 2010 !".

In the past COBOL has always been unsuitable to address a large number
of common programming tasks, like systems programming.  But that has
not stopped COBOL being the most used programming language in history.
It is clear to me that COBOL has been successful by being very, very
good at a fairly narrow range of tasks: business applications.  Why
should we think it is necessary, or important, or even desirable,
that COBOL suddenly try to do everything other languages can do?  In
the late 1800's machines to make paper clips were perfected.  Today,
not only are paper clip machines still made the same way, many are
the very same machines in operation over 100 years ago.  If you look
at a modern 'steam shovel', you will see that it is made virtually
identically (in form and operation) to the very first ones.  The
hammer has been virtually unchanged for a very long time.  Why?
Because some things do certain jobs so well that changes only make
them worse, not better.  How many times have we moaned because some
favorite application was 'over enhanced' to the point that it became
bloated, slow, and sometimes practically useless?  Do you think the
people doing the 'enhancement' were aware of the result as they were
doing the 'enhancing'?  Surely not, for if they were, they were
moronic to have done it.  I believe they become so focused on their
goals, they lose sight of the whole picture.  If you look at the
scope of the changes being made to the COBOL language in the draft,
it is enormous.  I am convinced to my bone marrow that we are now
witnessing this very thing being done to COBOL.  I really, really
hope I am wrong. :-(
```

```
the back side of our Swiss Army Knife.
```

```
This argument is 40 years old.  While the name is COBOL  where B is for
business, why is it necessary for business applications to use VB to create
output for monitors?  Because monitors were not available in 1960.  Why did
the ability to handle bits not get into COBOL '60?  By restricting the
scope, the arguments were shortened, and progress was made.  I don't know
about everyone, and I hate to tell on my self, but I am often required to
keep my views to my self because, the doors to minds are not open to my
views.  In my view, the concept behind COBOL was that it was very helpful
to write the program logic in the common language so that it would be
easier to understand with the benefits that come with that.  Still, I agree
that new languages will be coming down the pike, but anything good in them
IMHO will find their way into English (of the COBOL sort).  I have been
relatively quiet about this for 40 years, but face to face with people who
want to discuss this I have only two words, BS. <VBG>

Warren Simmons
w.g.simmons@worldnet.att.net
```

```
Cheesle wrote in message <8a5sci$nia$1@news.cerf.net>...
>I cannot see this being implemented as ANSI standard, as
>    a) ODBC is a vendor specific interface (read: Microsoft)
>and
>    b) is non portable.
>

ODBC is "vendor specific" only on the physical data side. The application
interface is generic; i.e. Your program uses the same source code regardless
of the physical data structure (same code works with Oracle, xBase, text
files, etc).

MCM
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8aeegl$291$1@news.cerf.net>`
- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com> <38c02889.69848657@news.transport.com> <q0h0cs8i632g3o546c5voii59eiojptgb5@4ax.com> <38C0B894.A2E482B5@home.com> <7j43cson8697j65qdemgviu001u28gnqge@4ax.com> <38C1F245.BCE36D2B@home.com> <jjt5cskgfmu26ko4etdpk04ivmpgnk24oc@4ax.com> <8a16i4$l5k$1@nntp4.atl.mindspring.net> <1398cskr7docc5o2cg8qccagqbu2tmaep5@4ax.com> <38C531C9.5378276@home.com> <qRax4.4645$5Y2.70748@news3.mia> <Zkgx4.2615$HN.138687@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <38C66090.83A1B7DD@cusys.edu> <8a5sci$nia$1@news.cerf.net> <nBby4.517$xL1.118415@dfiatx1-snr1.gtei.net>`

```
"Michael Mattias" <michael.mattias@gte.net> wrote in message
news:nBby4.517$xL1.118415@dfiatx1-snr1.gtei.net...
> ODBC is "vendor specific" only on the physical data side. The application
> interface is generic; i.e. Your program uses the same source code
regardless
> of the physical data structure (same code works with Oracle, xBase, text
> files, etc).

Not so. The ODBC mechanism is a four tier construction:

    My Application
        ODBC Client API
            ODBC Manager API
                Database

Both the ODBC Client and ODBC Manager is the property of Microsoft. It may
not be moved to any platform without their blessing. Hence, if it were to be
embedded in Cobol, and be as portable as Cobol, the Cobol vendor would need
access to the source of this stuff and the permits to do this. However, you
are correct that with this construct in  place, the Cobol would not need to
worry about what is in the other end.

I guess you just misunderstood what I said no to.

Cheesle
```

```
That's probably a bug in the driver or driver manager. The ODBC spec was

this arena , the "CLI" or "Call Level Interface.")

environment? Book not handy) attributes, you have specified you will be
using "ODBC-supported-SQL" or "DBMS-supported SQL".
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 2000-03-10T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Vr6y4.25825$wk.1368603@news1.mia>`
- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com> <38c02889.69848657@news.transport.com> <q0h0cs8i632g3o546c5voii59eiojptgb5@4ax.com> <38C0B894.A2E482B5@home.com> <7j43cson8697j65qdemgviu001u28gnqge@4ax.com> <38C1F245.BCE36D2B@home.com> <jjt5cskgfmu26ko4etdpk04ivmpgnk24oc@4ax.com> <8a16i4$l5k$1@nntp4.atl.mindspring.net> <1398cskr7docc5o2cg8qccagqbu2tmaep5@4ax.com> <38C531C9.5378276@home.com> <qRax4.4645$5Y2.70748@news3.mia> <Zkgx4.2615$HN.138687@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <38C66090.83A1B7DD@cusys.edu> <Hkyx4.41$lA4.1578@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```
Windows programs interface with printers using a printer API that
doesn't exist in real printers.  It is the job of the printer driver
to take the output from the program and map it to the particular
printer in use.  This seems to work quite well.  For GUI interface,
why could not a similar thing be done in COBOL?  Personally, I would
much prefer extensions to the Screen Section (or a new section) for
for this purpose.  Then the compiler vendor would be responsible
for writing the 'driver' for the particular OS GUI API.  Why should
every COBOL programmer have to learn all the ins and outs of all the
GUI APIs they will have to deal with?  No, this would not support
all possible features of any given GUI API.  But do business
applications *really* need all those little bells and whistles?
As I see it, the current approach being taken is analogous to how
printer drivers worked under DOS; every application had to interface
with every printer.  With the Windows printer API, this interface
need only be written once for each printer, and Windows programmers
no longer need concern themselves with all the details of every
printer out there.  Would this not be a far, far more efficient
solution?  COBOL programmers could simply learn the COBOL 'API' for
GUI screen handling.  This would *enormously* extend portability.
In my opinion this, and the greatly reduced learning curve for GUI
programming, would significantly outweigh the disadvantages. In
fact, if the Screen Section were updated cleverly, the compiler
could generate code to make it work with character interfaces, if
the application were written using certain constraints.  It also
would have the benefit of being a 'COBOLesque' approach, which the
current approach, using API calls to external routines, is not.
This is a C/C++ type solution, not a COBOL one.
```

```
I would have agreed with you until I tried it. The real problem is that it
only works in theory.  I just did a project that runs on about forty
installations.  There are about ten printer types, all lasers.  We have to
print customs papers, and the government is fussy about the form.  Stuff
that worked on one truncated on others, etc., etc..

Unfortunately, with something like a cheque, you CANNOT allow the format to
spontaneously change to fit the printer.  The bank will not cash it, the
government will make you hand copy it onto a standard form.  The fact that a
human can read it is irrelevant.

We did eventually sort it all out (with Bob Wolf's FormPrint), but there in
no way that I could see to do it with HTML (for example). It is just too
bloody arbitrary.  I will grant you your point of a few weeks back ... the
problem is that the software is just not where it should be yet.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 2000-03-10T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<N%9y4.26346$wk.1373476@news1.mia>`
- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com> <38c02889.69848657@news.transport.com> <q0h0cs8i632g3o546c5voii59eiojptgb5@4ax.com> <38C0B894.A2E482B5@home.com> <7j43cson8697j65qdemgviu001u28gnqge@4ax.com> <38C1F245.BCE36D2B@home.com> <jjt5cskgfmu26ko4etdpk04ivmpgnk24oc@4ax.com> <8a16i4$l5k$1@nntp4.atl.mindspring.net> <1398cskr7docc5o2cg8qccagqbu2tmaep5@4ax.com> <38C531C9.5378276@home.com> <qRax4.4645$5Y2.70748@news3.mia> <Zkgx4.2615$HN.138687@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <38C66090.83A1B7DD@cusys.edu> <Hkyx4.41$lA4.1578@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <Vr6y4.25825$wk.1368603@news1.mia> <38c91df3.10231938@news.epix.net>`

```
Bob Wolfe wrote:
>Judson McClendon wrote:
>>Windows programs interface with printers using a printer API that
>>doesn't exist in real printers.  It is the job of the printer driver
>
>Not sure what you mean by "real printers."

I mean that the Windows printing system was not designed to look like
any specific real printer.  It is an API 'target' printer that the
program uses as if it were real, then the Windows printer drivers
translate the printing commands into the actual commands sent to the
physical printer.  That way, the Windows programmer does not need to
know what kind of printers will actually be used with the program by
the end users.

>Other than the fact that it doesn't use a "screen section" approach,
>I believe that this is "kinda/sorta" what FormPrint does.

Yes, but it is not embodied in the COBOL language itself, such as
the Indexed Files capability is.  I'm suggesting that a standard
'COBOL GUI', be defined in the COBOL language, in a manner analogous
to Indexed Files, to provide one single GUI API that all COBOL
programmers could learn, and not worry about which O/S the program
would be run on.  Assuming the compiler supported both platforms,
the program could be ported unchanged by simply compiling for the
different platform.  The savings in programmer training alone would
be truly enormous.  I know is could be bad news for you personally,
but creating a COBOL printer API might be useful too.

I'm sure such embedded APIs would not be as flexible as FormPrint or
SP2, but those tools could be used when their extra capabilities were
required.  The very fact that SP2 and FormPrint provide such valuable
utility show that there is real need in those areas.  I would just
like to see them addressed by standard COBOL, rather than learning a
gazillion ever-changing operating system APIs. :-)
```

```
"Judson McClendon" <judmc@bellsouth.net> wrote in message
news:N%9y4.26346$wk.1373476@news1.mia...
> Bob Wolfe wrote:
> >Judson McClendon wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Windows Printing System (tm) was a retail and OEM product that
Microsoft sold for Win3.x and Win95. I don't think they sell it
anymore, but I get that you're talking about the Windows way of
printing.

> >Other than the fact that it doesn't use a "screen section" approach,
> >I believe that this is "kinda/sorta" what FormPrint does.
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> gazillion ever-changing operating system APIs. :-)

How to support the wide variety of printers out there: Adobe
with PostScript has advanced the notion that all minor printer
differences can be glossed over with the use of a very high-level
description of what you want to print -- that is, PostScript.
And, then, as long as every printer in the world is a PostScript
printer, you don't have a problem.

The Windows API for printing (similar to one the drawing on the screen,
and yet, different) allows the application to ask a buncha questions
like what fonts are installed, how wide would this sequence of
characters be, what is the resolution of the printer, and how much
memory does the printer have? These questions (or queries) usually stop
at the printer driver, but it's possible for the question to be
answered ultimately by the printer device itself. The Windows API has
high-level and low-level drawing functions but letting a real
application in a particular conversation with a real printer get real
answers about the printer's capabilities and parameters is the biggest
difference between a function-oriented and a data-stream-oriented
printing.

PostScript started as a data stream and was functionized into Display
PostScript.

The Windows printer API started as function calls and with metafiles
was data-ized into a data stream.

I'm not that familiar with what printing products are currently
available for COBOL, but the COBOL printing that I've done has been
line-printer-oriented. That is, ASCII (or carriage control) is the
printer language -- limited as it is. I imagine that more than a few
COBOL program embed printer-specific codes and thereby become
printer-specific themselves as well as more platform-specific (trusting
that the environment won't muck with the data as it passes from program
to printer).

Stephen
```

```
I don't think that a standard COBOL interface for printing would be
bad news for us.  The same was said about a standard GUI a week or two
ago.  We would be very supportive of a standard.  We sold tools
*before* Windows existed when many people were using DISPLAY and
ACCEPT code.  We not only generated the CALLs, we also optionally
generated DISPLAY/ACCEPT code.

If a standard were approved, we would simply create another set of
templates for the code generator in the GUI and printing products to
generate code for the standard.

We *would* still support the CALL interface, because many of our
customers would continue to use it....like the great philosopher Sly
once said...."Different Strokes for Different Folks!"

;-)

>I'm sure such embedded APIs would not be as flexible as FormPrint or
>SP2, but those tools could be used when their extra capabilities were
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>gazillion ever-changing operating system APIs. :-)

Thanks for the kind words!


Bob Wolfe, flexus
```

```
On Sun, 12 Mar 2000 19:06:46 -0800, "Cheesle"
<cheesle@online.NoSpamPlease.no> wrote:

>"Thane Hubbell" <thaneH@softwaresimple.com> wrote in message
>news:38cad045.245018762@news.cox-internet.com...
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I seem to be having trouble making the point.  The point is that you
are shooting at a target that is moving faster than you gun can aim.
What I mean is that what a GUI is changes FREQUENTLY.  You can't
standardize the GUI until you define what it is, and that is just
plain impossible at this point.  Let's say you do somehow succeed in
defining it - next week your definition will be obsolete.  It's just
something that is not really necessary.  For example:

With CICS the UI was (I say was because the UI is now the web in a lot
of places) BMS Macros.

With System 36, 38 and AS/400 it's SDA (Screen Design Aid).

With WANG its (proprietary, can't remember the name) a kind of panel.

All of these depend on the platform.  Asking the compiler vendors to
support all of these with their compilers, and all the changes that
come along is NOT in our best interest.  It will cause a slow down in
innovation, and will cause the prices of the tools to increase
dramatically.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** rtwolfe@flexus.com (Bob Wolfe)
- **Date:** 2000-03-15T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38cfae1b.10026908@news.epix.net>`
- **References:** `<38cd41fb.405225305@news.cox-internet.com> <20000313173248.08210.00000408@nso-fb.aol.com>`

```
sff5ky@aol.comxxx123 (Sff5ky) wrote:

>In article <38cd41fb.405225305@news.cox-internet.com>,
>thaneH@softwaresimple.com (Thane Hubbell) writes:
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>produce identical results as seen on hte development platform.

I agree that not too many vendors would be willing to support all
platforms, but Merant, Fujitsu, Acucorp, ICOBOL, IBM and RM do support
multiple platforms today.  Some of these companies only support
Windows and UNIX plaforms, but others support a much wider variety of
*very* dissimilar platforms such as Windows, UNIX, OS400, AOS/VS, Dec
VMS, DOS/VSE, MVS, etc.  Here's a small example of 2 dissimilar GUI
environments and the problems which the compiler vendors need to
consider:

1.  You write the new standard syntax for compiler A running on
Microsoft Windows.

2.  Your source code includes the standard syntax for a combination
box (drop down list field) which is pretty darn standard in the MS
Windows environment.

3.  A prospective customer wants to purchase your software, but they
use a UNIX Motif environment.  Compiler A doesn't support UNIX
Motif.....no problem, you use standard syntax.  You move your source
code to compiler B which supports the standard syntax and specifically
supports UNIX Motif.

4.  You compile and either get compile errors or your code just won't
run.....why?  Because the environment (Motif) doesn't have the
equivalent type of control.  If the compiler is specific to Motif,
then there is no reason for them to support syntax which isn't
applicable to their supported environment.

Sure, the Motif compiler vendor can modify their compiler to recognize
the combo box syntax and either ignore it or attempt to display some
sort of "workaround" control in Motif.

But, it will cost the compiler vendor more to account for these types
of controls which are supported on some platforms, but not on others.



Bob Wolfe, flexus
Check out The Flexus COBOL Page at http://www.flexus.com
```

```
Good point.  However we could moderate your request with Thane's point
and work for an interface which allows for changes and growth.  I have
mentioned the HTML/XML interface and the ODBC/JDBC interface.  An extra
layer can be useful here.  Sure, we may need to keep to the old way to
write forms and checks - but not necessarily.  Forms software can
receive our data.

I like having the business rules in the COBOL & in the database.  But I
strongly suspect that the I/O formatting can (to various degrees) be
removed safely.   A screen section shouldn't NEED to know whether you
are using X-windows, MS-Windows, or a 3270 terminal.  Let an
intermediate program do the adjusting (and return info to tell you that
your 3-D rendering of someone's face was not displayable...)
```

```
On Tue, 14 Mar 2000 07:29:18 -0700, Howard Brazee
<howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote:

>Good point.  However we could moderate your request with Thane's point

I see the point.  Perhaps something COULD be standardized, but the
level of standardization must be controlled.  This is off the top of
my head, but here's how I see it.

COBOL programs need data from the user and they need to present data
to the user.  Perhaps the communications section is where this should
be - except that it's now an obsolete item in the standard.  Let's not
standardize how individual screen widgets work - instead standardize
how this data is passed to and from the widgets such that COBOL source
is transportable.
```

```
Powerform is used for printed output.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_(reply depth: 23)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 2000-03-14T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<zPsz4.4513$_P5.1385316@news2.mia>`
- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com> <38c02889.69848657@news.transport.com> <q0h0cs8i632g3o546c5voii59eiojptgb5@4ax.com> <38C0B894.A2E482B5@home.com> <7j43cson8697j65qdemgviu001u28gnqge@4ax.com> <38C1F245.BCE36D2B@home.com> <jjt5cskgfmu26ko4etdpk04ivmpgnk24oc@4ax.com> <8a16i4$l5k$1@nntp4.atl.mindspring.net> <1398cskr7docc5o2cg8qccagqbu2tmaep5@4ax.com> <38C531C9.5378276@home.com> <qRax4.4645$5Y2.70748@news3.mia> <Zkgx4.2615$HN.138687@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <38C66090.83A1B7DD@cusys.edu> <Hkyx4.41$lA4.1578@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <Vr6y4.25825$wk.1368603@news1.mia> <38C92A24.E5366C07@home.com> <w6ay4.26363$wk.1373771@news1.mia> <8abc04$98$1@news.cerf.net> <8abgju$oa5$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <8aef62$32p$1@news.cerf.net> <38cad045.245018762@news.cox-internet.com> <8ahm0h$d0r$1@news.cerf.net> <38cd41fb.405225305@news.cox-internet.com>`

```
Thane Hubbell wrote:
>
>I seem to be having trouble making the point.  The point is that you
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>something that is not really necessary.  For example:

I believe the misunderstanding is that you view the *entire* GUI
capability being supported by COBOL.  Indexed Files doesn't support
*all* possible file handling mechanisms; it is designed with a very
useful, modest capability.  I suggest such an approach here, though
I would like to see a more complete approach than Indexed Files. :-)
The vast majority of business applications simply don't need very
fancy GUI capability, they can do fine with modest capability.  The
extra goodies could be added using 3rd party software, just as
database capability beyond Indexed Files can be added now.  Any GUI
should have the capability to have windows, or at least one window
(the whole screen), display text and graphics, and permit the user
to enter text, and probably make selections of buttons, boxes, etc.
If a given GUI doesn't support a specific feature, such as drop down
menus (if that were in the standard), then the compiler vendor could
add that capability in their runtime code.  So certain limited GUIs
would require a bit more effort for compiler vendors wanting to
support that platform.  The very fact that SP2 can easily port to a
number of different GUIs (as you have pointed out to me, and thanks :)
shows that a given set of functions (e.g. those supported by SP2)
*could* be embodied in COBOL.  In other words, you already use a
package whose very existence and success disprove your point that a
'Universal GUI' could not be built, or would be impractical. ;-)

Remember that applications are becoming more and more complex,
increasing development times, and useful system lifetimes are
becoming shorter and shorter.  When those times cross, you can't
get there from here.  What we need is *not* more and more complex
programming environments, we need to simplify, simplify, simplify.
In programming, we do it by abstraction, making one object or
function that does a whole bunch.  And make it *simple* and easy
to use, and *easy* to learn.  Your point about the rapidly changing
environments merely emphasizes my point.  We aren't getting smarter.
Programmers ten years from now won't be able to assimilate
information faster than programmers can now.  But look at the curve
of how much information programmers have had to assimilate to be
productive, over the last 20 years.  This cannot continue forever.
If we don't start working on making our programming tools simpler
and easier to learn, we are going to hit a brick wall, the limit of
how much information programmers can assimilate and learn to use,
within the available time, and still have enough time left to be
productive.  This needs to be done, and it needs to be done *now*.
I have watched in horror the last decade as the programming task is
being made more and more complex by the programmer's *tools* and
target environments becoming much more complex.  I have little
admiration for Steve Jobs, a man who not only trashes his enemies,
he trashes his friends too.  But he said something a few years ago
that rang true like a bell.  He said (paraphrase) "It is not to
Microsoft's advantage that software development become easier.  It
does not matter much to them how much it costs to develop a new
package, because when you sell millions of copies, the development
cost becomes a very small factor.  But it becomes very significant
for their competitors, all of whom have much less money, and sell
far fewer copies."  The Windows 32 bit API is vastly over
complicated, and as a result, programming for Windows is just as
over complicated.  And Microsoft compounds it by changing it all
the time.  After all, their people know ahead of time what the new
changes will be, and can prepare for them before their competitors
can.  This forces their customers to assume *enormous* retraining
costs, to the point that some companies are still on Windows 3.1!
If Microsoft will do this to their customers, they certainly will
do it to their competitors, many of whom we work for.  While it
might put money in Microsoft's pocket, it is insanity for the
programming community.  You *never* increase productivity by making
a task longer and more complex, and you *never* improve accuracy
that way, either.  As all experienced programmers know, humans are
very error prone (That means me and you, too!  Good programmers
have just learned to deal with and minimize it. :).  Having good
standards, so everybody must only learn something once, is a very
important adjunct in addressing the problem.  One primary lesson
the military has learned through vast experience over history is
that a commanding officer can only do so much, and beyond that
point, his effectiveness drops dramatically.  The concept of the
General Staff was created to address this problem, and it works,
well.  In essence, the General Staff makes the commanding officer's
work far simpler and easier than it was before.  He still must make
the important decisions, but he makes them at a higher level of
abstraction, and the details are carried out by his staff.  For
the military to accommodate to more and more complex wartime issues,
they had to make the commander's job *simpler*.  Since programmers
can't have General Staffs, we must address the issue by simplifying
in other ways, beginning with programming environments and tools.
But we haven't been doing that, we've been going in the opposite
direction.  Why do almost half (over 45%) of new systems being
developed fail to be implemented?  Over budget, over time, or
failure to meet design goals.  Twenty years ago this figure would
have been considered incredible.  It didn't come about because
programming was getting easier, it was because programming is
becoming more difficult.  Systems themselves are becoming more
complicated, by necessity, you say?  Even more reason to make the
programmer's job easier.  Plot the curve over time, and extend it
into the future.  I guarantee you that the growing complexity of
our programming environments will not go on forever.  I just hope
we learn to simplify them voluntarily before we hit the wall. :-)
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote:
> 
> Thane Hubbell wrote:
> >
> >I seem to be having trouble making the point.  The point is that you
> >are shooting at a target that is moving faster than you gun can aim.
etc.....

> Judson......
> I believe the misunderstanding is that you view the *entire* GUI
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> I would like to see a more complete approach than Indexed Files. :-)
...... 

Phew !I could have left the rest in - but you've said it. It just makes
bloody sense, as you well illustrated with your military analogy. So I
look at a flyer dropped off to-day, I can get Windows 2000 for $459.99
CDN - Why ? Exactly what is William G. offering me that I need. No, I
don't want to stifle forward development, and give the guy his due, he
has contributed significantly towards EDP - but making a lot of moohla
in the process. No problem say many COBOL developers, it's fantastic,
use Visual Basic, Activex whatever. Fine, but they all point down the
same yellow brick road - Microsoft - the de facto standard. 

Well I don't buy into the de facto bit - today yes, and maybe for
another decade - but history repeats itself - and just like Big Blue,
M/S will wane - most assuredly when Billy Boy retires from the helm.
Meanwhile those itsby-bitsy little competitors like Linux - God bless
'em.

Windows is as you say vastly over-blown, and using those tools which
genuflect to Windows - hey, no problem. In my own case, looking at it in
producing a workable 'business' system, I have found no need to use more
than the following :-

	- textfields
	- entryfields
	- pushbuttons
	- radiobuttons
	- listboxes
	- droplists		

Hey ! I could have used checkboxes - WHY ?

Six controls without going artsy-fartsy. The rest is 'Window' dressing.
Qualify that a bit, I'm not into the 'Net stuff - so that may require me
to introduce more controls - if I EVER get into the 'Net. And in my
application I grab the whole screen - you want to dabble with
WordPerfect or a Spreadsheet, then get out of my application and come
back to it when your mind has stopped leap-frogging. Similarly, just
don't bother to double-click - nothing will happen. I don't have any
mystery stuff lurking in the background - what is available is visual on
the screen, extra goodies being obtainable from a pushbutton.

Each little tweak in Windows can give you and me heartburn, and also
their competitors. I'm with you firmly - COBOL should be able to have a
standard 'Prime set' of 'Screen' controls not 'Windows' controls - with
the ability by a flag, switch, object reference, whatever, to address
the needs of a particular O/S. 

Jimmy
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_(reply depth: 25)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 2000-03-15T00:00:00

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Screen Section in the next Standard (was: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_(reply depth: 21)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2000-03-11T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8aeuqj$a0m$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com> <38c02889.69848657@news.transport.com> <q0h0cs8i632g3o546c5voii59eiojptgb5@4ax.com> <38C0B894.A2E482B5@home.com> <7j43cson8697j65qdemgviu001u28gnqge@4ax.com> <38C1F245.BCE36D2B@home.com> <jjt5cskgfmu26ko4etdpk04ivmpgnk24oc@4ax.com> <8a16i4$l5k$1@nntp4.atl.mindspring.net> <1398cskr7docc5o2cg8qccagqbu2tmaep5@4ax.com> <38C531C9.5378276@home.com> <qRax4.4645$5Y2.70748@news3.mia> <Zkgx4.2615$HN.138687@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <38C66090.83A1B7DD@cusys.edu> <Hkyx4.41$lA4.1578@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <Vr6y4.25825$wk.1368603@news1.mia> <38C92A24.E5366C07@home.com> <w6ay4.26363$wk.1373771@news1.mia> <8abc04$98$1@news.cerf.net> <8abgju$oa5$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <38c9d8fe.181706698@news.cox-internet.com> <Z5vy4.15103$aw3.243363@news2.mia>`

```
In reply to this and a couple other notes asking for my memory.  I am not
positive that I remember all the details, but I think that the following is
SOME of the current and past issues:

1) Having the Character based Accept/Display enhancements in this revision is
STILL one of the most controversial items.  Although it is "required" it is
also marked as "processor dependent" with an explicit statement that
implementers need not include it - for ANY reason that they don't even need
to document.  Several vendors and countries have already indicated that they
think it is "obsolete" technology that will be ignored.

2) The current character based Accept/Display (Screen Section) is based
CLOSELY on the X/Open specification (but not quite identical to it).  For
those who DO want it in the next Standard, there are a few relevant items:
   A) Any X/Open conforming implementation with a character based interface
(still medium common in UNIX environments) will be portable (almost)
immediately to the new Standard
  B) The new character based interface may be used a lot like the "old"
Accept/Display facility, i.e. for debugging and during application
development
  C) There were political reasons in '94 for trying to provide some of the
extensions from X/Open into the next Standard.  Although time has made these
less important, the features have remained. (Another example is the
file-sharing/record-locking capability that several vendors have also
indicated they will ignore in their proprietary COBOLs.)

3) The GUI interface variations of Screen Section were "rejected" because (at
that time - and even to this day) they were proprietary and had NOT been
adopted by any other vendor.  Therefore, there was question about how "user
acceptable" (much less user-demanded) they would be.

4) As stated elsewhere, there is really STILL no "Standard" for GUIs. In '94
when work on this revision was progressing, the situation was even more fluid
(with OS/2 still rivaling the "oft-delayed" Windows 95).  Therefore, the
committee did not think they could "standardize" on a single COBOL language
interface to something that itself was in such a state of flux.  Also, in '94
"X/Open" (and UNIX) were (often) viewed as synonymous with "open systems" -
and if this were to be the future for COBOL, then the state of GUIs was even
LESS clear there.

   ***

There may have been other issues, but I think that this gives a "taste" of
some of the background of why what is and is not in the draft for
Accept/Display/Screen Section is as it is.
```

```
process requires that there be a "consensus" among national body Standards
groups.

that has nothing to do with the question of WHY something wasn't added to the
draft of the next ISO COBOL Standard.

GUI interface (and the APIs or equivalent) that meets "your needs".  Even
X/Open which WAS limited to UNIX type environments really never could solve
this.
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>Joseph Katnic <josephk@iinet.net.au> wrote in message
>news:38CBA1F4.1AC2CF8@iinet.net.au...
…[53 more quoted lines elided]…
>this.

Bill:

I think that I can contribute a couple of simple examples which will
support your point.

Many people think that a GUI is a GUI is a GUI and this is simply not
true.  All one has to do is consider the Windows environment from the
early 1990's to present and it becomes quite obvious.

Nobody disputes the fact that the Windows GUI has become the "de
facto" standard for Graphical User Interface systems.

BUT....the Microsoft Windows GUI has *always* been and will continue
to be a "moving target."  It is certainly in Microsoft's best interest
to keep changing it (gotta have that upgrade which supports them thar
new whiz bang GUI widgets!).

Because it *is* a moving target, it would be a challenge (to say the
least) to design a standard for GUI syntax which would be at the very
minimum 2 yerars behind when it is finally approved.  Don't get me
wrong.....I would LOVE to see a standard, because I believe that it is
good for the COBOL language in general.  It *is* however a formidable
task.

Another example involves UNIX Motif versus Microsoft Windows versus
OS/2 Presentation Manager.  Now some might argue that OS/2 PM and
Motif are non-issues here.  Perhaps.  Based on our observations the
growth in the popularity of Linux will dictate an ongoing market for
the Linux GUI, which is based on Motif.  Will it be a huge market?
Probably not, but who really knows?

If you compare Windows versus Motif, you will find significant
differences in the two.  For example, Motif doesn't support the
equivalent of a Windows "combo box" and there is no such thing as a
Parent/Child Window relationship in Motif.

I can understand *perfectly* why Bill emphasizes the complexity of
coming up with a standard.  It would be a tough job indeed.


Bob Wolfe, flexus
Check out The Flexus COBOL Page at http://www.flexus.com
```

```
"William M. Klein" wrote:

AHH Yes,
But my method would not be to approach the standards body until the
Vendors had experimented with different approaches. Then the most
popular, as per units of compiler sold etc, could be declared the base
and the others can be made to accommodate that approach and transition
their users to the new standard.

Incentive for the Vendors to experiment and do a good job for their
users (US) would be that the most popular (in units sold) vendor gets a
jump on the competition in the standards race.

I suspect this happens in the real world with new technologies. You
don't set a standard for a new technology and then go and invent the new
technology, it's the other way around.
```

```
Sigh.  Bill, I suspect you agree with this, at least to some extent.
But when the process becomes more important than the purpose for the
process, things are Screwed Up.  Bad.

I know you have many times requested that more of us become involved
in the standards process, and I appreciate that.  But people are very
clever, especially about discerning when their input to others would
be welcome and/or have much effect.  I, and I suspect many others,
have known instinctively that their own input would be virtually a
waste of time.  The fact that very, very few of us have ever actually
been queried by the standards bodies about our opinions is a very Big
Clue.  Any company would be totally insane to market a new product,
or significantly change a successful product, without seeking real
input from real consumers.  I don't perceive that the standards bodies
actively solicit anybody's opinions, other than their own.  If I were
personally responsible for developing the new COBOL standard, you can
bet that I would do my best to solicit the opinions and desires of
the true COBOL consumers, COBOL programmers, before making a move.
The compiler developers are almost incidental in this, no matter what
they think.  Because without our collective choices to use their
products, compiler vendors are history.  Period.  Compiler vendors
need to be there for input on how practical or costly implementing a
certain feature might be.  But what business is it of theirs what
the features should be?  Does anybody think I tell my clients what
features they should have in what they pay me for?  Do you tell your
clients/users what they want, or do they tell you?  Suggest?  Yes.
Decide?  Ha!  The compiler vendors aren't the ones trying to make a
living *using* the things; we are.  The standards process, for COBOL
at least, is broken, probably beyond repair, at least for the
foreseeable future. :-(
```

```
and the purpose is not to show how clever we are and run off at the
mouth. We enjoy our language, get frustrated with lack of detail in our

are really not doing a good job.

position than we are.

formed into sub-groups, to tackle those specific modules of COBOL that
turn their crank. Start getting user input from people in this NG - and
if you are using the NG - then you should bloody-well care. Then we need
some mechanism where these joint thoughts can be submitted to J4, WG$,
```

```
I noticed that James J. Gavan as jjgavan@home.com said...
> 
> So here's a germ of an idea - and perhaps Bill can flesh it out - based
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> whatever, representing what we are after for the future.

Laudable idea.  However - yes, there has to a "however" - wherever I've 
worked very few, if any, COBOL programmers really care much about the 
language except when a change to it affects them; then most of them moan 
for a while and then adapt.  In mainframe environments, the humble COBOL 
programmer has little or no say as to which compiler is to be used, it's 
all decided by systems people who don't use it.

Thus, the average programmer is left out of the loop - they don't 
understand why changes are made, they don't understand why they are being 
forced into, or out of, certain practices and they certainly don't feel 
that any input that they might have will be treated with any respect.

This apathy is embedded in the psyche and it saddens me but, at the end 
of the day, most managers don't understand what's going on either, or 
they would allow (even encourage) discussion.
```

```
James J. Gavan <jjgavan@home.com> wrote in message
news:38CD224D.6A68EE85@home.com...

> So here's a germ of an idea - and perhaps Bill can flesh it out - based
> on any comments that come back - and I sincerely HOPE THAT THEY DO.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

I have said it before and I'll say it again.  It really REALLY does matter if
each individual (not a group) submits their ideas.  Getting them submitted to
J4 is as easy as sending an email note to

   doncobol <at> mediaone.net

and asking for the "idea" to be discussed at J4.

Getting it to the ISO group is more difficult, but usually does simply
require that you send an email or hardcopy note to your national body asking
them to share your input with the ISO SC22/WG4 COBOL Working Group.

P.S.  If (and only if) you would like me to "preview" any input to tell you
whether there might be "style" improvements that you could make to get it
better received, I would be happy to.  I would also be willing to tell you if
(to the best of my knowledge/memory) it had already been discussed BUT both
groups do change their mind so this would not be an attempt to try and stop
you from submitting your input.
```

```
Bill, I think the idea of everyone putting in their oar is
non-productive. Even assuming folks did write to Don, he really doesn't
want to be inundated with a 1,000 messages saying the same thing, or in
a slightly different flavour.

People in the group do have 'pet' issues - let them thrash a particular
interest around in a sub-group and see if they can come up with a
generally agreed idea as to how they see an improvement could be made.
Then, by all means, you could jump in, indicate previous history,
rejection or modification of the idea etc... Then they can discuss do
they still want to 'push' for this idea regardless of previous
rejections. Then by all means, don you Horace Rumpole outfit and flesh
it out for the folks so it is in a nice convoluted form for J4/WG4 to
read in a style they understand :-)

OK here's one for starters - I don't currently use Screen Section having
gotten into %%$$!! Windows tied in with OO - BUT - I am very sympathetic
to Judson's idea of a common set, (and I would suggest a limited set) of
'Screen controls' in COBOL Screen Section, which overrides Thane's
sensible observation, 'but we are looking at a moving target'. Cheesle
the AcuCOBOL 'dual screen' has to surely be the basis for what Judson is
after.

Short quote re the above, having quickly browsed a book which arrived 
to-day :-

'(a) - Keep design really, really simple. Cutting-edge controls are
great but.....  keep the cool and obscure controls to a minimum.....
(b) - stick to basics, don't go overboard shifting them around.....
(c) - use role model for consistency of style (looks like, feels
like)...
(d) - don't use a bunch of crazy colours.... 
(e) - At times desirable and highly appropriate to deviate from the 
norm (i.e. don't slavishly follow the Windows model).....
(f)....... and goes on an on........

The book ? Waite Group's Visual Basic 6 Super Bible - seems I've heard
that name before - isn't VB a Microsoft product ???
```

```
"William M. Klein" wrote:
> 
> James J. Gavan <jjgavan@home.com> wrote in message

> the draft) should make it clear where you stand on the following issues:


OK Bill - you are on - this is what I will write to Don :-

> 1) Do you think this next revision should be delayed until the GUI stuff
> could be added?

No <repeat> No - it should continue as currently planned. I would like
to see a new COBOL 2000X before I die ! Imperfect or not go with what
we've got. Don't delay the issue any further.
 

> 2) If the revision doesn't wait until the GUI portion is developed, do you
> think that the character based stuff should still be included?

Yes it should - because there are (as indicated in the NewsGroup) people
using character - off the top of my head, Judson, Richard Plinston and
Don experimenting, combining with Fujitsu OO.
 
> 3) If you are thinking of an "enhancment" for a future Standard (not the
> revision under work right now), then make that clear (and be aware that it
> will PROBABLY not even be looked at for a couple of years)

That's the bit that really bothers me - are you implying they already
have a shopping list (apart from OO and the 'Finalizer' problem) which
carries them through to May 2002 (basing that on Newbury this coming
May) ? 

> 4) What is your "view" on OO Standard class library versus
> Accept/Display/Screen Section support for GUIs (in some future Standard)?  Do
> you want BOTH - or just one and if just one, which one (and why)?

Tricky, tricky. People should not be 'forced' into using OO GUIs. Given
an adequate set of standard controls, and OO's ability to add additional
classes, then my preference would be for OO GUIs - but please compiler
vendors a VERY COMPREHENSIVE set of examples showing how to use the damn
things. But again, we shouldn't jeopardize text mode users.

Now having said that (Jeez, I'm sounding like you :-), what can we
anticipate as a result. OK I'll accept that Don is up to his proverbial
in alligators, first priority Newbury, but on a separate issue when can
Ken and I realistically expect a response to our volunteering to put in
our two cent's worth on OO ? Now don't tell me I've got to chase him to
remind him - because if so, then the bloody system is BROKE !

It would be interesting to know how the system works, so grade my
guesstimate on a rating from 1 - 10 :-

- (a) They use Roberts Rules of Order to conduct meetings - oh my gawd !
Washington DC yes - but in business ???

- (b) "Let's read and approve last minutes" - somebody nit-picks,
wanting to cross 'i's and dot 't's - a good half hour wasted, if not
more.

- (c) Every topic is followed with a proposed, seconded, show of hands

- (d) there's always one of them Bill - some jerk takes an issue and
takes the meeting off at a tangent

- (e) 'Physically' you meet very infrequently - hence you've got to cram
as much as possible into a one day session - and damn well feel mentally
drained when it's finished - and probably much is carried over as being
inconclusive.

- (f) - I sincerely hope that the J4 committee, as an example, doesn't
try to tackle every topic; if topics are not covered by sub-committees
you are on a hiding to nothing. There is no way the main committee can
absorb all the issues in COBOL - just consider that 'Finalizer' jazz -
not knocking people, but would suggest it truly is beyond the ken of
many - with a direct interest in the topic I had to read really hard
what those two Japanese gentlemen had written - and they seemed to know
their stuff.

So what's my grade teach' ? Pass or otherwise ? If you mark me high -
then I would suggest, before anybody does any more standardizing, that
they take a real serious look at how their organization is structured.

Hope your ears aren't plugged - I'll be 'bending' them at Newbury, given
half a chance :-)

PS: Judson - join the fray. Hell ! I'm happy with OO GUIs - it's you
that wants the Screen Section text :-)

Jimmy
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
> How would you implement a screen section in an IBM (or other vendor)
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> having two Cobols, PC Cobol and mainframe Cobol.

There should a base guaranteed set (similar to ISPF):

1.  Text fields
2.  Buttons to push  (where is cursor when enter is pressed on
mainframe)
3.  Menus that pop up. (mainframe / Windows top line X does it
slightly differently)
4.  Selection lists  (emulated on mainframe with PF key and careful
design).
5.  Screen close, minimize, windowing (simple split on mainframe as
per ISPF).
6. Standard messages:  Close, scroll up, scroll down, help.
7.  Window size information.
8.  Context sensitive help (where is the cursor).

True GUI would be a superset of this, image handling, mouse position,
etc.

The problems would be that Gui would handle a 90 character scrolling
screen field and IBM just would not work well.  Positioning tends to
be system specific (characters on MVS, dots on screen in windows,
etc.).

Thanks
Ken Foskey
http://www.zipworld.com.au/~waratah/
```

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2000-03-18T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8b0qas$jdf$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<8abgju$oa5$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <38c9d8fe.181706698@news.cox-internet.com> <Z5vy4.15103$aw3.243363@news2.mia> <8aeuqj$a0m$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net> <38CBA1F4.1AC2CF8@iinet.net.au> <8agoil$9ne$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net> <LZ6z4.34088$wk.1485580@news1.mia> <38CD224D.6A68EE85@home.com> <8aru5u$brf$1@slb1.atl.mindspring.net> <38D1A0BD.DCD3FEE@home.com> <8asc9b$loe$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <38D1E27E.8BE335D8@home.com> <38D24241.F2D5A696@cusys.edu> <tpi4dso5tsol3m09rms9v8ndpudnaotls5@4ax.com>`

```
This will be handled by the revision by the use of the Standard "conditional
compilation" facility.  It doesn't give you a way of "testing" which compiler
you are using, but if you set up your own "parameter" on the compile you can
(will be able to) code something like:

>>IF compiler = "mainframe"
     Perform xyz
>>ELSE
      Perform ABC
>>END-IF

This is not a run-time test of the compiler but actually tells the compiler
ONLY to compile (treat as if coded) the relevant part of your source code.
```

```
James J. Gavan <jjgavan@home.com> wrote in message
>
> > 3) If you are thinking of an "enhancment" for a future Standard (not the
> > revision under work right now), then make that clear (and be aware that
it
> > will PROBABLY not even be looked at for a couple of years)
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> May) ?

This comment definitely requires a response.  The "procedures" required to
move from the current draft to an approved Standard will take ALMOST all the
(meeting) time for the next 2 years.  There are both ANSI and ISO rules
concerning the processing of comments that are extremely time consuming.

Having said that, I do expect SOME initial work to be proceding on:

A) definition of what will be in the "Standard Class library" technical
report (I expect serious work on this to being in the 2nd half of 2001 - if
there are no surprises during the review process of the current revision).

B) There are BUNCHES of "interpretation requests" on the current ('85)
Standard that are still outstanding and J4 has "put on the back burner"
during development work on the revision.  I *think* that some time will be
spent on trying to clear these up - before the next Standard becomes
"official".

  ***

HOWEVER, it is still worth getting your ideas into J4 (and more importantly
into WG4) on what priorities you think should be next on their schedules).
There simply is a (relatively) long "lead time" - but if you don't submit
your ideas now, they will be too late for the next work.
```

```
"William M. Klein" wrote:
```

```
> 1) Do you think this next revision should be delayed until the GUI stuff
> could be added?
> 

This has been something I've been wondering about.  In the mainframe
world most shops I've seen are reluctant to upgrade compilers.  They
don't want to convert all existing programs (expensive to do and test),
so they end up with lots of versions (expensive to maintain).  

I think I need to get a better idea on how the world would accept
continuous upgrades vs being left behind because we will NEVER finish
developing technology.
```

```
I noticed that Howard Brazee as howard.brazee@cusys.edu said...
> "William M. Klein" wrote:
> > 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> so they end up with lots of versions (expensive to maintain).  

It was only the advent of LE that caused any major ructions like this 
and likely more to do with y2k than LE per se.  Before then, we just 
changed to a new compiler and ran the programs through it when they were 
changed or developed, thus most sites ended up with a fair mix of source 
types.  There was no need to recompile every program just to cater to the 
new compiler.
 
> I think I need to get a better idea on how the world would accept
> continuous upgrades vs being left behind because we will NEVER finish
> developing technology.

As you said, there's a real danger of ending up with two or more versions 
of COBOL.

There is one scenario that I plain don't understand.  It was obvious that 
the 3270s were only enhanced with GDDM for graphic presentations and that 
was, let's face it, not the best solution regardless of how appropriate 
it may have been when designed.  When Java came along, my thought was 
that we'd see a series of replacements for the 3270 terminal series with 
Java onboard and that we'd be presenting data to these new terminals that 
would be interpreted by the Java-based terminal speedily, efficiently and 
with something to satisfy the aesthete.

Instead, what I see in actuality is a proliferation of crappy VB-based 
GUI presentations on networked PCs that the ordinary users don't even 
like.  Why don't they like them?  First, the change has been imposed on 
them; secondly, they are invariably slower and occasionally less 
functional than the text-based 3270 screens that they had before; and, 
thirdly, the users find that their job takes longer all for the sake of 
something that is purely cosmetic.  OK, it's a wild generalisation but 
I've witnessed all three of these and usually concurrently.
```

```
Charles F Hankel wrote:
>
>Instead, what I see in actuality is a proliferation of crappy VB-based
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>I've witnessed all three of these and usually concurrently.

This is my observation as well.  This breakneck race toward 'GUI Only'
reminds me of (pardon me) a bunch of sheep, or lemmings.  "Everybody
says it is Always Better, so it must always be better!"  Reminds me of
the fashion industry. ;-)  No tool ever made by man has ever been
"Always Better".  Spending billions of dollars and untold billions of
manhours on cosmetics and flash is pretty drastic action, in my opinion.
I have yet to see a situation where a GUI approach to typical prosaic
business applications, such as entering payroll or inventory data, is
more cost efficient than a text based one.  Drop down menus, combo
boxes, and other features can improve training, but you can do a pretty
good job with character based interfaces too.  I made a character based
help system that allows the user to get online help on any field on a
screen, search tables of codes (by code or description, try that with a
combo box with 20,000 codes!), and select one which is automatically
placed in the screen field, etc.  You can drop it into an application
easily, and the users can update the help information on the fly.  And
nobody but nobody can move a mouse and click a button faster than they
can type a character; it takes at least ten times more time and energy.
I don't view the character based interface as obsolete, but as the
simple and efficient form of interface, with GUI being the more complex
and flexible interface.  By all means, use GUI where it is needed or
truly helpful, but don't abandon all character based interfaces just
because they aren't as flashy.  For many uses, GUI is not needed, and
actually less desirable. :-)
```

```
    Things might be better when dealing with "internal" stuff (Ie any
program that does not need to
be marketed), but I fail to see the problem with a "crappy" VB-based user
interface.

    Ask your marketing department which would they prefer to sell:
1. A fast, well written, stable character mode program that does everything
the user wants.
2. A slow, unstable, buggy GUI that is low on features.

    They can probably make 10 times as much money on the GUI.

    Before you flame, I agree with the writers below, personnally.  However,
if marketing can not sell ...
```

```
although I have been involved in packages as well.  My point, as with 
Judson's is that a GUI is fine for some applications but for systems used 
by people who know what they're doing, the text-based system is hard to 
better.

themselves, or not "expert" users.  More often than not, decision makers 
have little or no experience of the job in hand and are easily swayed by 
buzzwords and window-dressing.

>     Before you flame, I agree with the writers below, personnally.  However,
> if marketing can not sell ...

True - I just wish that more actual users were asked before management 
makes decisions that affects them.  Still, it's not a clear blue sky 
every day, is it?
```

```

Charles F Hankel <charles@hankel.mersinet.co.uk> wrote in message
news:MPG.133eb7784a43b1a698999e@news.mersinet.co.uk...
> I noticed that RUSSELL STYLES as RWSTYLES@COMPUSERVE.COM said...
> >     Things might be better when dealing with "internal" stuff (Ie any
> > program that does not need to
> > be marketed), but I fail to see the problem with a "crappy" VB-based
user
> > interface.
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> better.

There are several benefits to GUI front-end systems.

The GUI application tends to be (semi-)intelligent. The I/O traffic may be
reduced because the user is prevented from entering incorrect, or
inconsistent data by the terminal.

There are limitations to how much data can be processed on a single screen.
A Customer data entry process may have 4 screens in a text based system. A
GUI application can handle all the data in one shot. This should reduce the
number of modules needed to process the Customer data.

> >     Ask your marketing department which would they prefer to sell:
> > 1. A fast, well written, stable character mode program that does
everything
> > the user wants.
> > 2. A slow, unstable, buggy GUI that is low on features.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I worked on a Purchase Order package for the vendor.
I would meet with the User Group Steering Committee at least twice a year.
We would discuss possible improvements to the system, and set priorities for
their implementation. ALL the members of the committee were users of the
system (Purchasing Managers, or Accounting). Believe me, they were not
swayed by window-dressing. They wanted features, and functions that would
improve their job performance.

Having said that, I should also point out that software marketing has
decided to move up the food chain when trying to sell products. If the
person they are targeting isn't smart enough to solicit the opinions of the
system users, they deserve what they get.
```

```
DennisHarley wrote:

>> RUSSELL STYLES wrote:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>There are several benefits to GUI front-end systems.

I don't think any of us are questioning this.  The issue is whether
or not GUI should be the *only* approach, or would many applications
work as well, or better, with character mode interfaces. :-)

…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

or character based interfaces.  After all, if you put the intelligence
to accept and validate data on the client PC, then why could it not
have a character based interface to do the job?  Right now, it would
only be because of a lack of character based tools. :-)
```

```

Judson McClendon <judmc@bellsouth.net> wrote in message
news:Ol6B4.108$l34.9414@news3.mia...
>
> The above points are more a function of system architecture than GUI
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> --
A character based interface can be GUI.
There is no requirement to use Drop Downs, Spin Controls etc.
In many instances they are unnecessary, and sometimes impractical.
You don't want a drop down list for 250,000 customers.

I do character based presentations in CICS. It is a royal pain to change
screen maps.
Programming for getting data in bits and pieces is more challenging than it
has to be.
If I can process all the data in one shot, I can concentrate on the business
logic, rather than the administration of 4 related screens.

A good VB, C++ etc. programmer can produce quality presentations of data
much faster than I can code CICS maps, and the supporting programs. I can
pass a Customer Master record to the desktop, and let them present it 100
different ways.

I don't believe that we should place the enforcement of business rules on
the desktop. Data has to be verified before it is placed in the system. The
verification should be done at  the data storage level. If the files are on
a server, use the facilities on the server to provide a final check that the
data is good.
```

```
James J. Gavan wrote:
>
> PS: Oh yes - and while I am it - REMEMBER gentlmen - to help William K.
> I started this thread on the basis that we should ALL get involved with
> the standards - that includes YOU down in Alabama too :-)

Beyond what I believe is the obvious need for COBOL based GUI, I had
not thought a great deal about what the COBOL syntax would look like.
But I am beginning to think it might be simpler than some here have
suggested.  I have not seen any AcuCOBOL GUI code, so pardon me if I
am reiterating their approach.

Starting with the Screen Section as it is in the new draft standard,
I don't see why it could not be implemented as it is in GUI.  This
would be a boring GUI, but pretty straightforward to implement.  And
beyond that, of course, you would want to assign additional properties
to items on the screen (relative font size and positioning based on
screen size, associated event handling code, etc.), and allow for
graphics, both drawn (lines, circles, etc.) and pictures.  I think
most of this could be handled as additional option phrases in the
Screen Section.  But the event handling might be better specified in
the Declaratives, or some other facility made for the purpose.  If
this would be sufficient, then it could be integrated with only a
reasonable amount of thought and effort.  There is already much
experience in what is required for a GUI interface.  Creating COBOL
syntax and semantics to support a modest, or perhaps not so modest,
GUI capability seems fairly straightforward.  Now I will don my
asbestos pants and wait for the storm of rebuttal. :-)
```

```
TSO/SPF has a drop down menu option in text mode.  I don't know anybody
who uses it, but it is there.  ADSO has a slightly better design for
generating maps and dialogs - programmers don't have any choice, they
have to use those drop down windows.  Colors are available for mainframe
programming, as are italics (which italics sometimes cover up the next
non-italic character).

What I haven't seen is ability to keep focus on different windows, but I
don't see why that can't be emulated.  And of course, we don't have the
ability to load graphics such as 3-d windows.  But since everything is
text, everything can be cut and pasted into an e-mail.  (some Windows
error messages cannot be cut and pasted as text - it is a pain to  write
it down in pencil just to type it in again into an e-mail).

Forgetting whether these emulated windows features are useful - it may
be worth considering what can be emulated in a text window.
```

```
On Tue, 21 Mar 2000 07:47:34 -0600, "Judson McClendon"
<judmc@bellsouth.net> wrote:

>  Creating COBOL
>syntax and semantics to support a modest, or perhaps not so modest,
>GUI capability seems fairly straightforward.  Now I will don my
>asbestos pants and wait for the storm of rebuttal. :-)

FLAME ON!  Oh wait, that's from a comic book.  

I'm still not convinced it should be standardized at all.  If we
standardize a limited GUI then this will be seen as a limit because
it's COBOL.  Thus COBOL should not be used.  By leaving the interface
entirely external this will not be a valid complaint about COBOL.  I'd
rather see it absent than half way.  

I find my applications in direct competition with other desktop
applications.  I need to get the users attention to get sales.  A
boring text interface does not do it - ignoring the argument that it
might actually be a BETTER interface.  I need to be able to use ALL of
the features in my target platform.  Now, I know that wether the GUI
is standardized or not, I will have the GUI I need because of the
tools I will continue to use.  However, the PERCEPTION among our peers
will be that COBOL is limited because of the half baked GUI
implementation.  We don't need that.
```

```
Thane Hubbell wrote:
> Judson McClendon wrote:
> >Creating COBOL
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> implementation.  We don't need that.

No one is suggesting that your ability to use your current methods
be curtailed. :-)  But why should everyone who could benefit from
a standardized COBOL GUI capability be penalized, when those of
you who want will still (and always) have the same options as you
do now? :-)  Dont throw the baby out with the wash. ;-)  And COBOL
has *always* been thought of as limited, and it is - intentionally.
We also willingly limit ourselves to carefully using structured
(and/or OO) techniques because of the benefits.  We willingly don't
use GO TO.  Limitation is not necessarily a disadvantage, especially
when the 'limitation' is actually specialization, as is the case
for COBOL.  Specialized tools are generally more efficient than
general purpose tools, because their specialization (limitation)
permits a more focused approach.  If nothing else, specialization
gets rid of the clutter of seldom used capabilities, something we
need In A Big Way in our GUI tools!  Every GUI programmer has at
some time or other bemoaned the complexities of GUI programming. :-)
Also by your same logic, Indexed Files capability should not have
been added to COBOL, because it wasn't sufficient for every database
need. ;-)
```

```
Thane Hubbell wrote:
> 
> >If so, then we may be better off WITHOUT a screen section - but WITH an
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> it one step furhter removed than Judson does!

Let's analyze this in business terms.  What do the users want and what
will they want over the long term?

They want reports.
They want to do data entry.
They want to change their reports, to massage their data, and to
summarize on different criteria.
They want real-time access to data.
They want real-time updating of data.
They want their order system to know whether or not the product being
sold is in stock.
They want audit trails.
They want automatic ordering of low supplies when a product is removed
from stock.
They want invoicing, pricing, etc.
They want security.

This is a starting list.  Some of these needs are solved on the central
computer (server or mainframe, who cares?).  Some of these are solved on
a PC (spread sheet).  So far, if our COBOL can talk to databases and to
ODBC, everything is hunky dory.  Then we have the real time needs:

A salesman scans a product code on a scanner.  The program checks the
price, marks inventory down one, checks to see if more products should
be ordered, checks the credit card, offers the good customer discount,
asks if the customer wants batteries, calculates taxes, and prints out
an invoice.  This looks like a job for OO.  What tools are best for the
interface?  Screen sections?  HTML/XTML generators?  CORBA objects?

You just wrote the above programs, and your company decides to do the
same thing on the Web.  What do you have to rewrite?
```

```
On Tue, 21 Mar 2000 15:08:30 -0700, Howard Brazee
<howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote:

>Thane Hubbell wrote:
>> 
…[39 more quoted lines elided]…
>same thing on the Web.  What do you have to rewrite?

Oh boy.  You hit my hot button.   BECAUSE I externalized everything
using SP2, I CAN DO THIS ALREADY.  I can take an application written
using sp2 and without changing a line of source, relink it and have a
"Thin Client" application.  Next, with some minor source changes
because Web browsers do not support all the windows controls, I can
make it an HTML application.  Not a line of business logic needs to
change.

Now, how this fits into the standard.  Unless vendors are going to
have screen sections that generate XML/HTML, and offer TERMINAL
EMULATION wherever there is a screen section display/accept - they
cannot get to one common interface WITHIN the language.  By
externalizing it with calls to an external system, you CAN easily do
all these things.  However, when it is embedded within the syntax of
the language, things like Thin client become very very hard to do, if
not impossible - the interface is back to being part of the actual
business logic.
```

```
common objects - without sharing source code.
```

```
Thane Hubbell wrote:
> 

> >You just wrote the above programs, and your company decides to do the
> >same thing on the Web.  What do you have to rewrite?
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> business logic.

Externalizing the interface certainly is the way to go.  We don't care
if it is a moving target.  We don't care if we change platforms.  This
is sort of like the logic behind TCP/IP - layered interfaces allow us to
write the part we care about, and plug in the part that someone else has
already developed for the other platform.
```

```
I'm certainly not against standardizing an ODBC-type section.  But
we can separate disk or screen I/O now, with separate programs.  I
don't see why any of this would be impacted negatively having a GUI
Screen Section.  I believe you guys are looking primarily at the
'extreme end', where applications must do all kinds of GUI tricks
to make sales.  No problem with that, but remember that COBOL has
always been a mostly in-house language, and by far the most code is
written by IS or consultants for internal use.  Unless you have some
reason for pushing the envelope, a reasonably vanilla GUI capability
would be all you need for virtually all those applications.
```
