```
Jimmy,

Fujitsu PowerCobol, which has been around for about 2 years
now (maybe more?) is basically what this thread has been
discussing.

You build GUI elements using a WSIWYG editor.  You then
write cobol code for each of the "events" that occur when a
control is clicked, changed, etc.

Each event is its own standalone
mini-program (called a "scriplet").  Code that would be duplicated
in multpile events is simply put in its own scriplet, and CALLed
by the individual events.

Each scriplet also has its own local variables.  Global variables
may also be declared, which are useable from within any
of the scriplets.

Its still good old cobol.

The WSIWYG editor actually generates cobol code to define
the elements, but you don't have to do the "row#, column#,
raised.." as Cheesle mentioned.  That stuff is in there, but
you don't have to look at it.

Earlier versions of PowerCobol were very buggy.  The one
they give away for free, V3, is one of those early versions.
If you really want to build something commercial, don't bother
with that version.  But then, you didn't expect to get a
solid compiler for free, now....

The current version is very stable, and does *exactly* what
most of this thread "wishes" for in the future.  What it doesn't
do, of course, is set a "standard".  Much if it is specific to
this product, and the learning curve is *very* steep.  It has
taken me a full year to get my productivity back to the level
it was with previous character-based cobol products (I've been
playing this musical instrument for 20 years).

If you've ever used VisualBasic, this product will feel very familiar.
The graphical setup tools are an exact copy.  I tried to learn
VisualBasic a few years ago, and spent a lot of time going
nowhere.  Cobol is very good for what we do (lots of I-O,
error-checking, the usual business stuff...).  VisualBasic is
much harder to use for those applications.


don ferrario
http://www.ferrario.com
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote:
> 
> Thane Hubbell wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> would be all you need for virtually all those applications.


Originally as a result of something you wrote earlier I was going to
comment as to what Thane's reaction would be - he beat me to it !
Although I find the 'sub-set' idea of a Screen Section containing GUIs
extremely attractive - Thane is right. He doesn't need a half-baked
approach - simply all or nothing, and we know the former is unattainable
because 'Windows is a moving target' due to M/S marketing generating the
suitable consumer demand.

Consider the on-again off-again situation with COBOL R/W - has to be a
minority who use it, (and I'll acknowledge from my perusing years ago
it was a good tool). Assume we arrived at a COBOL 'sub-set' of GUIs
containing 30 controls deemed really necessary. In view of Bill K's
comments elsewhere in this thread, how long would it take for COBOL to
catch-up. Closer to home, M/S now allows for left, centre and
right justified with single line entryfields - I'm still waiting for
Merant to play catch-up with that one little detail in the Dialog Editor
!

One thing we should remember when we get into Microsoft Windows bashing
- Bill Gates didn't start it - it's been a natural progression, where he
has come out current victor. (Not all progression is naturally good - I
enjoyed some of the earlier days of TV when we looked at a poky little
black and white screen - looking at most of the junk on TV now I would
prefer their colours were grey on grey !)

OK so there was Simula first, then Smalltalk. Can't find the exact
reference, but the feisty young Adele Goldberg (reminisces of Grace),
whisked a few 'desktops' out the front door, and gave kids at a local
school the chance to play with 'figures' on screens. The experiment
being a success, then she and her accomplices asked for formal
permission :-)

Now continue the story in Adele's own words :-

"1979 was year of ominous events......Steve Jobs then head of the
fledgling Apple Corporation visited PARC - twice. First a courtesy visit
hosted by Xerox. Then Jobs came back for a second demo ... with the
entire Lisa programming team. He and the Apple programming team waited
three hours... while I argued in vain that a demo for the programming
team was an inappropriate give-away. Worse, Job's insisted that I give
the demo, knowing I was the group's teacher. My manager ordered me to
give the demo, claiming later that he was ordered to do so from
corporate. The plans for Lisa were revised..........."

OO Programming Languages 1998.

Doesn't need spelling out does it - Bill G. latched on to what Steve was
doing and gave us Windows. Now it was Steve's turn to be PO'd.
So come on Linus give Bill's tree a real good shake !

Jimmy
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL Standards : was Screen was Structured v OO

_(reply depth: 43)_

- **From:** Joseph Katnic <josephk@iinet.net.au>
- **Date:** 2000-03-22T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38D8E06A.A85D3D9C@iinet.net.au>`
- **References:** `<38C66090.83A1B7DD@cusys.edu> <8abc04$98$1@news.cerf.net> <8abgju$oa5$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <38c9d8fe.181706698@news.cox-internet.com> <Z5vy4.15103$aw3.243363@news2.mia> <8aeuqj$a0m$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net> <38CBA1F4.1AC2CF8@iinet.net.au> <8agoil$9ne$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net> <LZ6z4.34088$wk.1485580@news1.mia> <38CD224D.6A68EE85@home.com> <8aru5u$brf$1@slb1.atl.mindspring.net> <38D1A0BD.DCD3FEE@home.com> <8asc9b$loe$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <38D2415F.B5BE44EB@cusys.edu> <MPG.133ca2ee483236e4989984@news.mersinet.co.uk> <yhLA4.43105$wk.1679781@news1.mia> <8b11lu$ah8$1@ssauraaa-i-1.production.compuserve.com> <MPG.133eb7784a43b1a698999e@news.mersinet.co.uk> <ejZREFak$GA.268@cpmsnbbsa02> <Ol6B4.108$l34.9414@news3.mia> <38D6F4DC.6732DF07@home.com> <V4LB4.2999$z22.234622@news2.mia> <38d79192.1080588118@news.cox-internet.com> <38D7CC51.C4459DC1@cusys.edu> <38d7df87.1100548057@news.cox-internet.com> <d0TB4.47179$wk.1867140@news1.mia> <38D86623.D72D8A57@home.com>`

```

I have been reading this thread and I think that most people are missing the
point about "GUI SCREEN" I-O.

There are a couple of standards around now that can be used to map function
onto the native GUI drivers for whatever system you are running on.

Firstly there is "Display Postscript"
This can be used to define the layout and content of the screen/window etc.

Then there's "Display PDF"
Apple has started using a variant of PDF to display all of it's windows. This
this has the advantage that any screen or window can be captured to a PDF
document.
This function is purportedly being incorporated into the OS X operating system.

Finally, there's good old HTML (the thing that started it all off).
Currently, the only way a program can format a screen via HTML is to be invoked
by a WEB Server. It would be really nice if one or all of the above could be
invoked by COBOL program directly. Maybe some direct pipe to NetScape or IE? Of
course you could use
LOCALHOST to send the HTML to Netscape and imbed a Web Server in the Driver code
that the COBOL program would use for HTML.

I envision something like:

Identification Division.
Program-id. Blah.
Environment Division.
Input-Output Section.
    Select Program-Window
      Assign to HTML-PROCESSOR
      Processing Mode is Server|Application
      Window Status is Window-Status1 Window-Status2.
Data Division.
Window Section.
WD  Program-Window
    Value of CONTENT-TYPE is Program-Window-Content-Type
             CONTENT-LENGTH is Program-Window-Content-Length
             PATH-INFO is Program-Window-Path
    Data Records are Program-Window-Form1
                     Program-Window-Form2
                     Program-Window-Data.
01  Program-Window-Form1.
    05 Input-Field-1   PIC X(20) Varying Window Name is "INPUT1"
    ...
01  Program-Window-Form2.
    05 Input-Field-2   PIC X(30) Varying Window Name is "INPUT2"
    ...
01  Program-Window-Data PIC X(150) Varying.
  
Procedure Division.

    Open Program-Window for I-O.
    Read Program-Window into
         Program-Window-Form1 When Program-Window-Path contains "PATH1"
         Program-Window-Form2 When Program-Window-Path contains "PATH2".

    ... (logic)
    
    Move "Content-type: text/html" to Program-Window-Data.
    Write Program-Window-Data.
    Move Spaces to Program-Window-Data.
    Write Program-Window-Data.
    Move '<!doctype html public "-//w3c//dtd html 4.0 transitional//en">' to
         Program-Window-Data.
    Write Program-Window-Data.

    ...
    You get the drift.

Now, whilst the above is for a HTML windowing system.
The Window type could be changed to ADOBE-PDF or DISPLAY-POSTSCRIPT or whatever
other markup language you think would be usefull (note: this works for producing
reports as well).

Then the logic (Procedure division) would need to be changed for each type of
markup language. 

Once we had this type of feature, we could write a generic module that could
call a driver module based on the type of display system being used.

Then (and this is the good bit) it would be up to the programmer to decide what
level of dumbness the display's would have to enable the code to work with
different markup scheme's. Any given markup scheme should be implemented
identically across platforms (sort of like NetScape on WIN, Unix, etc).
```

```
"James J. Gavan" wrote:
> 
> I get the gist of what Joe is saying, also following through on his
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
OK,
What I'm suggesting is that you forget the GUI standard as GUI standard, and
concentrate on a markup standard (SGML, HTML, XML, Display or otherwise
Postscript, PDF format, etc.)

Then the compiler vendor, would be free to take these standards and implement
them in a GUI for whatever platforms they choose to support. This implementation
would be part of the runtime supplied with the compiler.

I would avoid (like the plague) charging for this runtime (increase in compiler
charge ~ OK).
```

```
i would skip that as well. standardizing markup in a compiler is akin

mixing languages. you can have many different tools and source code
which connects to markup code, but to force it on each implementation
is bad news. sample code and calls, not standardization.

source, and writes out cobol code skeletons, creation of
picture/plugin files, a search engine, and credit connection code.
```

```
This is two in a row...  I agree.

After seeing the hoops that we have to go through to get accurate
presentation for online bills on both explorer and Netscape (let alone
opera, ...) HTML is not the standard to work with yet.  It really is a
bugger to work with unless you have support routines.

PDF is only an output strategy (a good one mind you, but proprietary).

XML has promise in that you cobol program would accept and output a
data stream without consideration for format.  The screen format sits
outside the program, not really what we are discussing here.  This is
still a fairly young standard though.
```

```
G Moore wrote:
> 
> this code looks bad to me. yes, standardization is nice in *theory*.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Fonts and presentation is/are what the Markup languages handle.
Why the program should be bothered with them is beyond me.

The program should confine itself to the details of the logic of the
problem being solved. Other things can beautify the resulting output.

Your argument is like the old days.
"What do you mean standard IO for disk datasets? Disks will change
geometry's, how can I optimize the access, etc. etc."

I think it's about time we took the GUI out of the '60s and into the
new millennium. The BEST way to do that is to form a standard that
allows for flexibility under the covers.

P.S. IBM has used a Markup Language "DTL" to help programmers design
ISPF 3270 screens for some time now.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL Standards : was Screen was Structured v OO

_(reply depth: 42)_

- **From:** "Cheesle" <cheesle@online.NoSpamPlease.no>
- **Date:** 2000-03-22T00:00:00

```
"Judson McClendon" <judmc@bellsouth.net> wrote in message
news:V4LB4.2999$z22.234622@news2.mia...
> suggested.  I have not seen any AcuCOBOL GUI code,

I could not resist, please bear over with me, but here is a quick'n dirty
sample, using frames, radio buttons, labels, entry
field, check box and push button.

All this may also be accomplished with traditional DISPLAY/ACCEPT for each
individual field.

You can use BEFORE, AFTER, EXCEPTION procedures or EVENT driven programming,
the choice is yours.

01  demo-screen.
        03  frame-1, frame, "Color", raised,
            line 6, col 9, size 15, lines 10.

        03  radio-button, "Red", line 9, color red,
            col 11 group 2, group-value 1, visible 1,
            event procedure is change-app, id RED-ID,
            using color-button-value.

        03  radio-button, "Blue", line 10.5, color blue,
            col 11 group 2, group-value 2,
            event procedure is change-app, id BLUE-ID,
            using color-button-value.

        03  radio-button, "Green", line 12, color green,
            col 11 group 2, group-value 3,
            event procedure is change-app, id GREEN-ID,
            using color-button-value.

        03  label, "Name:", col 13, line 7.
        03  label, "Address:", col 11, line 9.
        03  label, "Phone:", col 12.5, line 13.

        03  entry-field, col 18, line  7, size 20, using user-name.
        03  entry-field, col 18, line  9, size 20, using user-address-1.
        03  entry-field, col 18, line 11, size 20, using user-address-2.
        03  entry-field, col 18, line 13, size 20, using user-telephone.

        03  check-box, "Default Configuration", line 8, col 12
            using button-value.
        03  push-button, "Change Configuration",
            size 23, line 10, col 12, ok-button.

        03  push-button, "Save Changes",
            line 10, col 19, size 15, cancel-button.
```

- **From:** sff5ky@aol.comxxx123 (Sff5ky)

```
In article <ejZREFak$GA.268@cpmsnbbsa02>, "DennisHarley"
<LegacyBlue@email.msn.com> writes:

>
>There are limitations to how much data can be processed on a single screen.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Exactly what makes you think that a GUI application can handle more 
data per screen?  If you are operating within the boundaries of a single 
screen view, both GUI and character are limited to how much space is
available and the 'clutter effect' of trying to get too much done in a single
response.   If you are referring to being able to scroll down a page in the
GUI presentation, it is nothing more than multiple pages slung together
with a single transmit/send/enter point.  This creates a much larger region 
to have to back pedal for corrections and relationship based field editing.
Maybe I'm on the wrong track?
```

```
Sff5ky <sff5ky@aol.comxxx123> wrote in message
news:20000319180348.06344.00000826@nso-fu.aol.com...
> In article <ejZREFak$GA.268@cpmsnbbsa02>, "DennisHarley"
> <LegacyBlue@email.msn.com> writes:
>
> >
> >There are limitations to how much data can be processed on a single
screen.
> >A Customer data entry process may have 4 screens in a text based system.
A
> >GUI application can handle all the data in one shot. This should reduce
the
> >number of modules needed to process the Customer data.
> >
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> available and the 'clutter effect' of trying to get too much done in a
single
> response.  If you are referring to being able to scroll down a page in the
> GUI presentation, it is nothing more than multiple pages slung together
> with a single transmit/send/enter point.  This creates a much larger
region
> to have to back pedal for corrections and relationship based field
editing.
> Maybe I'm on the wrong track?
>
Take a look at a tabbed dialog. Try the desktop properties on windows 95,
98, NT.

A GUI presentation doesn't handle more data per screen. It handles more data
per transmission.
The screen can be less cluttered, by breaking the presentation into smaller
chunks.

It also reduces the number of transmissions, since the user can navigate
through the pages without  communication to the server.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL Standards : was Screen was Structured v OO

_(reply depth: 37)_

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com>
- **Date:** 2000-03-20T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38D6B090.5E52F9D1@home.com>`
- **References:** `<ejZREFak$GA.268@cpmsnbbsa02> <20000319180348.06344.00000826@nso-fu.aol.com> <#YiK96mk$GA.254@cpmsnbbsa02>`

```


DennisHarley wrote:
> 
> Sff5ky <sff5ky@aol.comxxx123> wrote in message
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> chunks.

Dennis'es approach is correct - BUT - you can make a screen hold more. 
Consider an old DOS application - table of 10 by 12 values - reason - 10
columns was all I could get across the screen, and 12 lines down having
prettied it up with one of the DOS screen painters. What I put on the
screen 'forced' the design of the data file - 10 x 12 table with a
sequence key if more than 12 lines. Never tried it but I recall you
could change screen mode to 40 ?? - but if you did the 'smallness' would
soon make the user go blind !

User 'assures me' he will NEVER want more than 24 lines in Windows - so
I've created a screen(dialog) with 10 columns x 24 lines. Didn't quite
fit to show pushbuttons - so made this particular screen a 'child' of
the desktop not the applications's Master Menu. Use a systems font for
bold clear display of values, (again he ain't gonna go blind), disable
lines/columns not particular to this record; it is uncluttered
concentrating in one table on what the user wants. Nearly forgot, my
file record is now ONE (10 x 24).

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
the "observer" list. (Both DEC and DECUS - the DEC user group were on the
committee when development of this revision began.)

HP is on the committee and one of their user groups (I think) is now an
active "liaison".

Unisys is on the committee - but it is only "one half" (and I don't remember
which) of the original two COBOL vendors.

CA-Reallia was on the committee several years ago (and had a person fairly
active in the VERY early OO development).  Again, they have no current rep -
but do "observe" the activities of the committee.

The Fujitsu rep is a person from Germany (he TALKS to Siemans - but actually
came from an IBM background.)  As far as I can tell he neither talks to the
US or Japanese Fujitsu developers or planners.  I do know that when he is
asked about their current product and standard conformance, he "admits" to
not being involved with the actual Fujitsu product line.

The complete current membership list (including observers) for J4 (not WG4)
is available at:
```

- **Message-ID:** `<xpCy4.29817$wk.1428344@news1.mia>`

```
Cheesle wrote:
>Ken Foskey
>> Mostly they are already abstracted.  X-windows and Gates Windows being
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>misunderstood, it is just like it is today.

I have obviously expressed myself poorly, possibly by using the term
'API'.  What I am suggesting is built-in COBOL language support for
GIU in a similar manner to the way character based UI is being added
in the Screen Section.  This would consist of COBOL constructs, made
for the purpose, to become part of standard COBOL syntax.  I envision
something like an enhanced Screen Section and additional Procedure
Division statements and options to current statements (e.g. ACCEPT
and DISPLAY).  Once COBOL programmers learned to use this built-in
COBOL capability, they could write GUI applications without having
to learn additional GUI APIs or GUI libraries, just as they can now
learn Indexed Files and use that on any computer without having to
learn anything else.  Learn once, use anywhere, save amazing amount
of time and energy. :-)
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote in message ...


>I have obviously expressed myself poorly, possibly by using the term
>'API'.  What I am suggesting is built-in COBOL language support for
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>learn Indexed Files and use that on any computer without having to

I agree with you 100% on the goal, but I am not sure that I agree on your
methods.  The approach that I am taking is quite simple.  I want a few
simple objects that I can populate a screen with, and write the type of
Cobol program that I want.  I have it down to about 4 basic objects.

I then plan to write a validation suite for those objects, that ensures they
are completely abstracted from the Cobol mainline, and write all my
aplications using them.  The objects themselves will be written in Cobol,
using whatever platform specific tools I have available.

I also plan to write my own "GUI" design tools as mainlines, using those
same objects.  One goal is that existing screen sections must be able to be
converted mechanically to use the same tools, by as simple a mechanism as
changing DISPLAY SCREEN-NAME to INVOKE "display" using "SCREEN-NAME".
After all, a screen *is* an object, and each "from/to" field on it is also
an object.

In theory, I can then convert anything in the collection to any platform
that has the tools to write the original four objects.


>learn anything else.  Learn once, use anywhere, save amazing amount
>of time and energy. :-)
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
"donald tees" <donald@willmack.com> wrote:

>I agree with you 100% on the goal, but I am not sure that I agree on your
>methods.  The approach that I am taking is quite simple.  I want a few
>simple objects that I can populate a screen with, and write the type of

i can think of a few objects i would want. everything is call by
reference, except values are only modified if they are read. i just
typed this up today. it's a general template, but it covers
practically any object.


02 picturebox.
	05 loc.
		15 x pic 99999.
		15 y pic 99999.
	05 horizontal-pixels.
	05 vertical-pixels.
	05 online-memory	usage pointer.
	05 procedure-table.
		10 mouse-event.
			15 up usage procedure-pointer.
			15 dn usage procedure-pointer.
			15 loc occurs 5 times
					indexed by previous-point.
				20 x pic 99999.
				20 y pic 99999.
	
		10 load-file	usage procedure-pointer.
*wipes online memory
			15 filename	pic x(80).
			15 use-type pic 1.
				20 link-info usage
					procedure-pointer.
*such as the dll filename, offset to the procedure, ect.
					25 at-x.
					25 at-y.

*copies masks from at-x to end-screen-x and at-y to end-screen-y
*and replaces upon dll exit.


		10 wp.
			15 write-pixel	usage procedure-pointer.
			15 x	pic	9999999.
			15 y	pic	9999999.
			15 color.
				20 r	pic 9999.
				20 g	pic 9999.
				20 b	pic 9999.	
		10 wl.
			15 write-line	usage procedure-pointer.
		10 arcs.
			15 write-circle	usage procedure-pointer.
			15 write-arc	usage procedure-pointer.
			15 params.

02 sound-event.
	15 filename	pic x(80).
	15 time-offset-to-picture.
		20 set-seconds-from-0	usage 	
					procedure-pointer.
		20 return-seconds-mod-60-from-0 usage 
					procedure-pointer.
		20 data		pic 9999999v9999.
*final sound is displayed upon completion

	15 set-mute		usage procedure-pointer.
		20 mute	pic 1 		value 0.
		20 not-mute pic 1 	value 1.

	15 volume	usage procedure-pointer.
		20	minimum	pic 9999999.
		20	maximum pic 9999999.
		20	start	pic 9999999.

02 menu.
	15 location.
		20 x pic 99999.
		20 y pic 99999.
	15 add-item	usage procedure-pointer.
	15 remove-item	usage procedure-pointer.
	15 set-item-unreachable
			usage procedure-pointer.
	15 set-item-reachable
			usage procedure-pointer.
	15 levels	pic 9.
		20 from-top	occurs depending on levels.
*first level doesn't count
			25 menu-item pic x(10).
*case-insensitive

02 listbox occurs 3 times indexed by last-refresh-lb-info.
	12 set-location usage procedure-pointer.
		20 x pic 99999.
		20 y pic 99999.

	12 type-2.
		15 item-header pic	x(40).
*		case-insensitive
		15 item-hidden-specs.
		15 add-item		usage procedure-pointer.
		15 remove-item		usage procedure-pointer.
		15 on-click		usage procedure-pointer.

	12 set-sort-order	usage procedure-pointer.	

02 text-box.
	12 loc.
		14 at-x pic 9999.
		14 at-y pic 9999.
	12 event-x-ym-char.
		14 linkage.
			20 char pic x.
*or for win ce pic xx
			20 x pic 9999.
			20 y-minus-scroll-bar-offset pic 9999.
		14 procedures-set-then-execute.
			15 type-on-focus
				usage 	procedure-pointer.
			15 highlight
					usage	procedure-pointer.
			15 set-char
					usage procedure-pointer.
			15 read-char	usage procedure-pointer.
		12 refresh-text-box	usage procedure-pointer.	
*			or display
			15 	set-y-offset pic 99999.
*				beginning at 1

	12 font-info usage procedure-pointer.
		20 linkage.

	15 chars-inside-box.
			20 x pic 999.
			20 y pic 999.
	
	15 sb.
		20 x pic 1.
		20 y pic 1.
		20 set-scrollbars	usage procedure-pointer.

*add additional textbox code standard cut / paste template
*from read / set letters

02 frame.
		15 last-display-info usage procedure-pointer.
			20 first-frame pic 1.
			20 replace-mask occurs 3 times indexed by
				previous-frame.
				20 at-x.
				20 at-y.
				20 horiz-pixels.
				20 vert-pixels.
			20 set-num-objects usage procedure-pointer.
				25 num-objects pic 999.
			20 object-set-loc occurs depending on
					num-objects indexed by

current-object-starting-at-1.

02 bars.
*used for scrollbars or bars
	20 loc usage procedure-pointer.
		25 at-x.
		25 at-y.
	20 set-direction usage procedure-pointer.
		15 set-size usage procedure-pointer.
			20 x pic 1 value 1.
			20 y pic 1 value 0.
			20 pixels-x pic 9999.
			20 pixels-y pic 9999.		

		20 count pic 999999.
			20 set-current-count usage

			20 set-total-count usage
```

```
I am starting with a much simpler list. I want to start with the absolute
minimum, and bootstrap it from there.  The absolute minimum, as I see it,
should emulate a simple screen section.

length
width
Cobol picture clause of data
Cobol picture clause for accept/display
enable flags for above
row
column
background/foreground colour
link to next
link to previous
return value for mouse click
unable flag for above
type-(one of three) screen, field, button(command).
actual data
Cobol data name

Not every object would have every one of those attributes, but every object
could do with that minimum. Every other type is an adaptation of one of
those three with extra attributes.  The minimum set would yield a text only
object oriented interface, but it could be full oo design, completely
testable for business logic, and the code itself could write a minimum set
of screen update routines.  That is what I am aiming at, anyway. I am
envisioning a sort of Forth, starting with "Cobol objects".
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote:
> 
> Windows programs interface with printers using a printer API that
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> why could not a similar thing be done in COBOL?  Personally, I would

The windows API is all about generalizing the screen output :-}

Programmers program to interfaces.  The interfaces should move from
platform to platform seamlessly.  The true results might not be an
exact match but functionally the same.  This is the void that SP2 fits
in.

That method could be external to the compiler vendors and be a public
domain one.  We can tune the underlying code to the platform that it
is currently being implemented on but not change the interface.

Thanks
Ken Foskey
http://www.zipworld.com.au/~waratah/
```

```
Ken Foskey wrote:
>Judson McClendon wrote:
>> Windows programs interface with printers using a printer API that
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>The windows API is all about generalizing the screen output :-}

Yes, but it is not embodied in COBOL syntax. :-)  I'm talking about
defining a 'COBOL GUI API' *within* the COBOL language itself,
supported by Screen Section and Procedure Division syntax.  This would
prevent us having to learn the GUI API of the 'O/S du jour'. :-)
The Windows API is also *enormously* complex, with many times the
number of 'bells and whistles' actually necessary to write reasonable
business applications.

>Programmers program to interfaces.  The interfaces should move from
>platform to platform seamlessly.  The true results might not be an
>exact match but functionally the same.  This is the void that SP2 fits
>in.

Yes, but SP2 is not supported as a COBOL feature in the language
itself, but as a 3rd party add-on.  No criticism of SP2 intended,
of course. :-)

>That method could be external to the compiler vendors and be a public
>domain one.  We can tune the underlying code to the platform that it
>is currently being implemented on but not change the interface.

Yes, but in that case, it would be only another of many different
external APIs already present, and would actually compound the
total number of possible APIs a programmer might have to deal with.
I'm talking about a 'COBOL Standard' GUI interface, defined in the
language itself, just as are Indexed Files are now.  Once that COBOL
API was learned, just as Index Files are learned, the programmer
would not have to learn another GUI API to switch to another O/S, or
deal with Bill Gates latest brainstorm. :-)
```

```
On Fri, 10 Mar 2000 10:50:00 -0600, "Judson McClendon"
<judmc@bellsouth.net> wrote:

>
>Yes, but SP2 is not supported as a COBOL feature in the language
>itself, but as a 3rd party add-on.  No criticism of SP2 intended,
>of course. :-)
>

It uses a completely standard COBOL inteface - Call Using.

AcuCorp does what you are talking about with the Screen Section
presently for GUI - and it's NOT standard COBOL.  It's a nifty idea,
and AcuCorp is having some success, but you are slaved to their
compiler with it's other deficiencies.  Sp2 has no run time fee, and
uses COBOL syntax.

>Yes, but in that case, it would be only another of many different
>external APIs already present, and would actually compound the
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>deal with Bill Gates latest brainstorm. :-)

It's an interesting idea.  However, I would argue on the side that
EVEN ADDING THE SCREEN SECTION is wrong.  The User interface will
ALWAYS depend on the platform.  GUI does NOT mean Windows.  Features
availble on one platform won't be available on another.  Trying to
make one foot fit in all those shoes is unreasonable.  The user
interface belongs to the PLATFORM not to the source language.  I don't
think that the screen section will get widespread use after the
approval of the COBOL standard, I think it's already on its way out.  

What I DO strongly recommend, and this is where this message fits the
thread - is that we SEPARATE the business logic from the user
interface logic - OO is a nice way to do this.  

One CAN make a "standard" set of interface routines, that can be
extended for the particular OS using Inheritence and overrides
(polymorphism).

All in all, I've looked at them all.  Dialog system (proprietry
scripting language), Specific languag extensions, Special TypeDef's
and Headers, and wierd Working Storage and Call extensions to allow
native interface to the API to what I find is the simplest approach:
Call .... using .....

Standard, External and Simple.  Simple is good.  It is my hope that I
can make OO simple for me.  That will be good.
```

```
Thane Hubbell wrote:
>Judson McClendon wrote:
>>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>It uses a completely standard COBOL inteface - Call Using.

Ahem.  Begging the question a bit, eh?  The calls themselves (the
arguments, functions, etc.) are not standard COBOL. ;-)  Not that
the approach to SP2 is not a good one, or that it can't be
implemented in standard COBOL.

>AcuCorp does what you are talking about with the Screen Section
>presently for GUI - and it's NOT standard COBOL.  It's a nifty idea,
>and AcuCorp is having some success, but you are slaved to their
>compiler with it's other deficiencies.  Sp2 has no run time fee, and
>uses COBOL syntax.

It *would* be standard COBOL, and you would *not* be slaved to a
particular compiler, if there were such a thing defined in the COBOL
standard. :-)

>>Yes, but in that case, it would be only another of many different
>>external APIs already present, and would actually compound the
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>approval of the COBOL standard, I think it's already on its way out.

You are taking too narrow a view. :-)  You are looking at the O/S
interface as the 'user interface'.  Yes, that is the 'user interface
for the *O/S*.  But the 'user interface' to the application belongs
to the application, not the O/S.  How many different things do people
need to do in a user interface for business applications?  The user
interface necessary for effective business applications is only a
tiny subset of that provided by say, Windows.  The Windows GUI API
is massive overkill for COBOL business applications.  All COBOL
would need is a relatively modest GUI interface capability that
allowed data entry and display of textual and graphic output.  This
would be sufficient for the vast majority of situations, and you
could handle the others with API CALLs, SP2, etc.  The Indexed File
capability is a truly modest standard, with no READ PREVIOUS, and
missing many other common database capabilities.  But even so, it
has been extremely effective in many business applications.  I am
not suggesting the GUI capability be that limited, but it need not
be extensive. :-)

>What I DO strongly recommend, and this is where this message fits the
>thread - is that we SEPARATE the business logic from the user
>interface logic - OO is a nice way to do this.

Even so, programmers still have to program the things.  Why not
let the compiler vendor do it only once?  Repeatedly doing any
function that can be standardized and done once is a very high
price to pay for flexibility.  You need to know it's really
worth it.  In this case, I don't think so. :-)

>All in all, I've looked at them all.  Dialog system (proprietry
>scripting language), Specific languag extensions, Special TypeDef's
>and Headers, and wierd Working Storage and Call extensions to allow
>native interface to the API to what I find is the simplest approach:
>Call .... using .....

You still must learn to use different functions for CALL USING for
each different O/S.  If there can be a standard set of then that
would be portable, then why not embody them in a COBOL standard,
supported by syntax, so everybody could learn just one interface?

>Standard, External and Simple.  Simple is good.  It is my hope that
>I can make OO simple for me.  That will be good.

Why not make it simple for everybody, and save you the trouble? ;-)
```

```
Judson:

The called module is indeed a C language dynamic link library, but the
parameters which are passed are COBOL working storage variables.

>>AcuCorp does what you are talking about with the Screen Section
>>presently for GUI - and it's NOT standard COBOL.  It's a nifty idea,
…[63 more quoted lines elided]…
>supported by syntax, so everybody could learn just one interface?

I can't speak on non user interface issues here, because we only
support UI with sp2, but it *is* true that the sp2 supports the
identical function parameters are used regardless of operating system
or platform (text to GUI).  We hold the philosophy that you shouldn't
have to change your source code when moving from one platform or
compiler to another.
```

```
Bob Wolfe wrote:
>Judson McClendon wrote:
>>Thane Hubbell wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>parameters which are passed are COBOL working storage variables.

This is true.  But what the parameters are, and which functions are
supported, and what they do, are not part of the COBOL standard.

>>You still must learn to use different functions for CALL USING for
>>each different O/S.  If there can be a standard set of then that
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>compiler to another.

Bob, I believe the fact that you have done such a good job doing what
you describe above with SP2 is a very significant argument that the
same thing could be done by creating new standard COBOL syntax for this
purpose, with the compiler supplying the same or similar functions that
SP2 does now.  As with SP2, the COBOL source would remain the same
across platforms, only requiring a recompile.  Except for the fact that
that it would undermine SP2 sales over time, it seems like a good thing.
Not that I really think this is going to happen, but if it did, perhaps
you could license your technology to compiler vendors.  Less revenue
per sale, but *lots* more sales. :-)

As it stands now, I think SP2 is one of the best ways to go. :-)
```

```
entirely. i could easily write out a linkage section for fonts... you
```

- **From:** Ken Foskey <waratah@zip.com.au>

```
Thane,
  As stated a few months ago, it is true that "CALL" is a Standard feature of
COBOL - but it is *not* considered "native language" support for a feature.
Otherwise, you would have to say that the "low-level" Windows APIs are JUST
as "Standard" as sp2's approach.

I am NOT saying that either AcuCOBOL's or sp2's (or anyone else's) approach
is "better" than another - but saying that an add-on that uses the CALL verb
provides the functionality via "Standard native language" support just misses
the point.

P.S.  If anyone wants me to dig up my explanation of "alien" syntax, versus
"APIs" versus "native" language support (any of these via Standard or
non-Standard syntax), I can, but do feel that this is an important
distinction to rememberr.
```

```
Thane Hubbell <thaneH@softwaresimple.com> wrote in message
> What I DO strongly recommend, and this is where this message fits the
> thread - is that we SEPARATE the business logic from the user
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> Call .... using .....

I fully support Thane on this. The CALL ... USING ... is standard,
simple and portable. You hide ALL system dependent stuff
(UI, File access, sleep n millisecs, etc) in other programs that
you simply call. These other programs could be COBOL or C++
or anything else you can call from COBOL). The experience
we have with ETK shows that this approach works. We have
supported application consisting of millions of line of code
that are completely (100.00..%) portable to any platform with
a minimal COBOL compiler. The programs that do UI, File
Access, etc are not portable, but are quickly rewritten for
a new platform (or in case of File Access even generated
automatically).

Bottom line: hide ALL your system dependent stuff in other
programs and CALL them from the programs that contain
the business logic. Don't rely on the compiler, or OO, or
the vendor, or anybody else, to do this for. It is simple to
do it yourself. And it works.
```

```
Thane Hubbell wrote:
> 
> >Yes, but in that case, it would be only another of many different
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

The concept of public domain code that becomes a standard is quite
important.  The standard template libraries were defined by HP in the
early 80's today a vendor would not think of releasing a C++ compiler
without porting STL, most code would not run on it.

The advantage of a public domain interface is that it really is source
code.  If you have a platform and the vendor thinks that you want
x-windows for Unix but in reality you want terminal support then you
can do it.  If you want to extend the interface and hand it back to
the common good you can.  With proprietary implementations you have
not visibility of this.

Open source is the way of the future.  Competitive advantage of having
all the world looking at and enhancing your solution is significant. 
We could encourage our vendors to open up their interfaces to allow
this sort of thing.  Companies could sponsor open development, say a
telecommunications company wants an additional feature in the standard
code, they could pay an expert to do it and it becomes available to
all.   This model works exceedingly well for Linux with many companies
sponsoring open source work.

Thanks
Ken Foskey
http://www.zipworld.com.au/~waratah/
```

```
I just found this posting below in my files and was prompted to ask if
the GUI interface for COBOL should be a web browser.  The interface
specifications are cross browser and cross operating system.  While they
are a moving target, they at least aren't controlled by a single vendor.
Also COBOL should probably support the use of XML.  Doing both of these
things would mean a learning experience for me because I understand
neither of them but they look like a supportable way to go.  I will be
interested in seeing opinions on whether this is a valid approach.
```

```
This has been pretty much my position.  I also want to access ODBC/JDBC
with data & with meta-data.  COBOL is about business rules and data,
features which improve its ability to be clear and accurate with these
are very welcome.

But don't get bogged down in GUI details, there are other products which
are much better tools at hitting this moving target.  Tools such as
browsers, spreadsheets, or data mart tools should supplement our
business orientation.  So make it easy to link to those tools, and keep
Cobol doing what it does best.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard.brazee@cusys.edu>
- **Date:** 2000-03-07T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38C53F0A.1FEAB9D1@cusys.edu>`
- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com> <38c02889.69848657@news.transport.com> <q0h0cs8i632g3o546c5voii59eiojptgb5@4ax.com> <38C0B894.A2E482B5@home.com> <7j43cson8697j65qdemgviu001u28gnqge@4ax.com> <38C1F245.BCE36D2B@home.com> <jjt5cskgfmu26ko4etdpk04ivmpgnk24oc@4ax.com> <8a16i4$l5k$1@nntp4.atl.mindspring.net> <1398cskr7docc5o2cg8qccagqbu2tmaep5@4ax.com> <38C531C9.5378276@home.com>`

```
"James J. Gavan" wrote:
> 
> Now back to OO - and can't help feeling you have your eyes really closed
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> the same in structured, but it is infinitely more flexible in OO.

The difficulty will be in convincing me that I will be needing to write
programs to draw shapes.  Or more realistically, that those techniques
will be profitable in the type of programming I will be doing.

I will be providing data to people on PCs.  They will read the data with
standard tools such as browsers, data warehouse tools, and Office.  I
will be retrieving data from people on PCs.  But these procedures are
very similar to my old procedures of using files and printers.

If I want to draw a picture or even write a web page, I don't need to
know HTML nor angles anymore, I use a tool which doesn't require such
knowledge (just as in the old days, I didn't need to know what code was
in my printer to let 133 hammers hit the right letter at the right
time).  New tools allow my web page to access my data in standard
formats.

The places where OO has promise for me is when I create an application
based upon terminals, and then add voice response and Web screen
scrapers to those screens.  If I change the screens wrong, those other
applications could fail.  OO might be part of a solution here - and then
again, a better solution might be to use some nifty data warehouse tools
as an intermediate layer.  I don't know.

I can find lots of tools to do things I am not doing now.  We have to
know the tools so that we can know what we can do.  But then we should
decide what we SHOULD do, and make our decision based upon business
needs, not based upon what is fun nor what the tool can do.  I don't
want to be the early MacIntosh user writing ransom notes with all the
fonts possible (remember that?).
```

```
Forgive my butting in here.

My personal "example" is a FACTORY that manages the "JCL" for a string of
related program modules.  In my mind, it removes the need for a vendor
designed method for controlling setup, and tear down, distribution,
storage, and recovery for both test and production.


To use this factory, a program uses "something" that tells the FACTORY what
is needed, what messages may be needed, what timing factors are present,
how many copies, etc.

I know this is gross, but it's something that has been done via a separate
compiler, and the source for it was also created by the application
programmer(S).

Warren Simmons
w.g.simmons@worldnet.att.net
```

```
Pete Wirfs wrote:

> Most literature I have read on the subject suggests that it can take
> old procedural programmers (like me) several months longer to be
> trained and become productive than programmers that were brought up
> on OO concepts.  I believe they are correct.

I reviewed an article that effectively said that the fastest way to OO
anything from cobol was first to learn OO cobol and then learn OO
something else.  The reasoning was simple, remove the confusion of
different syntax from the confusion of OO.

You must be lead there, in my opinion, because there is far to much 'I
am doing OO' by programmers that have little real appreciation of what
it means.

A classic example is would you let a programmer fresh out of
University build a complete system without help?  You might understand
the syntax but understanding the complete picture is something else
again.
```

```
I have been using COBOL for 15 year's & fail to see the benefits of OOP.

The main argument seems to be code re-use but, I already have this with over
300 standard copy files & 187 callable routines that provide custom accept/
display commands, string formatting, windowing, & full application base set
of users/terminals/menus/printers/ etc..

I consider my-self to be an OLD programmer while at the same time have
no problems writing OO code. My choice not to use OO is down to the
performance hits in execution speed & the final generated size of the RT's.

I feel OO does have a place but not within file based transaction
processing.

PS. I'm so sad that I still enjoy coding in assembler...
      Oh, & is year 4000 definitely not a leap year ?
```

```
I have been coding in COBOL for over 20 years, and most of the COBOL programs I
have seen have been poorly designed from the beginning.

Programs that were designed for one narrow purpose, with no room for expanding
that role, HAS been expanded with so many fixes and patches that sometimes it is
unrecognizable as a COBOL program.  I have actually seen a comment on some code
that said ï¿½temporary fixï¿½ with a date ten years old!  Many programmers I have
known thought that ï¿½top-downï¿½ coding meant that they started at the top of the
program, and went to the bottom.

From what I have seen of the OO concept, its main focus is design on the front
end, which is what COBOL programmers should have been doing all along.  But now,
the OO people come along like knights in shining armor delivering the Holy Grail
called their new ï¿½paradigmï¿½ and claim that they have discovered that the key to
writing good programs is to have good design on the front end (NO DUH?!)
```

```
For Mr. Reed - just in case I get categorized into your 'knights in
shining white armour' - YOU CAN IMMEDIATELY CROSS ME OFF YOUR LIST.

Simply, my user had this bug in his mind to get into Windows and all
that good stuff - swapping between packages etc. He had me going around
like the proverbial blue-assed fly, looking at different COBOLs.
Eventually Micro Focus publicized VISOC at $800 per crack. Hey great, I
like M/F documentation - let's give it a shot.

Jeepers ! What is all this crap ! It offered the following permutations
:-

- Use Screen Section
- Windows APIs with structured
-  ..      ..  ..    OO  - not sure I'd have to check with Ken Mullins
-  OO and OO GUIs - I don't think you can segregate the two 
		could be wrong - but you need former to do latter 

So here I was stuck with a brand new baby - OO/GUI and attempted to code
in it, bloody awful fashion and only a modicum of success. Had I known
about Flexus SP2 I would have ditched the M/F product pronto.

However I persisted, and as I struggled along, things began to click in
a minor fashion. The OO bit DEFINITELY clicked when I got books from 
objectz.com. Now F@@##$$!! Windows - still a large mystery in many
areas  - but even that SOB I've partially learned to tame - using the OO
to make the GUI creating that very, very much easier.

A bloody long time - and unacceptable in a commercial installation -
although with peer-swapping of info, the learning curve has to be
drastically reduced.

Well, I'll tell you what - it works for me and saves me having to
anticipate some future cock ups - BECAUSE it uses that very old
technique reusability and brings it slap bang up-to-date with the
emphasis on flexibility.

Now whether or not you want to stay on the sidelines is purely your
choice - but for me after 'blood, sweat, toil and tears' it DOES work.
And at 69 I'm much 'too young' to be taken in by some come-on for a new
fad.

And on this fad stuff I have one book entitled 'Pitfalls of OO
Development' - seems everything can be a pitfall, with one exception -
files - the idiot author doesn't mention them - so I 'spose he's got
something that runs without a hard drive, diskettes or a CD ???

Jimmy
```

- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com> <38c02889.69848657@news.transport.com> <8a3bl6$hjj$1@newsg3.svr.pol.co.uk> <38C545D6.43D1A3B9@bellsouth.net> <59ebcsk5gnvi49glksgq4fnm18jktii2j0@4ax.com> <38C6E3D6.766B328B@home.com> <38C724AC.D14DF4A4@bellsouth.net>`

```
William Reed <adamreed@bellsouth.net> wrote:

>Why do you think the new computers need so much RAM?? It's so that entire files can
>be put into memory and manipulated there--faster access time, don'tcha know? :-)

actually, here's the reason i see them needing ram. if you double the
os from 16 to 32 bits, you get less ram than before, because now the
pointers take up twice as much space. also, if the instruction set
does not allow you to use the new data size in 2 16bit chunks, then
you essentially get half the ram you had in the 16 bit system. let's
pretend some manufacturer writes it for the 32 bit data types, and
then you change the specs so it handles 2 16bit chunks. do you think
the manufacturer will change it back for a few megs of ram for the
user? naw, fuck them.

that's just one factor. let's pretend you have graphics drivers
expanded. your previous 8bit plane becomes 16 or 24bit planes. that
means all the graphics double (unless the device driver is rewritten
to handle the 32 bit set).

and if the application instructions take up twice as much memory,
other than seek time, they take twice as long to load.

reply to email gvwmoore@spam.ix.netcom.com remove the spam
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

```
G Moore wrote:
> 
> William Reed <adamreed@bellsouth.net> wrote:
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> maintain it as easier than procedural.

The first is that the up front design is a requirement of any
programming.  All to often it is not done.

The graphics example is bad because it is not something that we
business programmers would do.  It is a clear case on how it would be
applied.  The same can apply to a dealer trading system, replace shape
with trade and away you go.


The OO paradigm gives you a closer mesh to the business, for example:

Actors: the users and interfaces of the system.
Process:  description of a normal task
exception:  description of an abnormal task

For example:

Actor:  customer

process:
Selects goods
goods entered on register
swipe card
return card and baggy of goods
smile sweetly

exception:
payment declined
	give sour sneer
        suggest finding another card
payment declined twice
	return goods to shelf.

This style of up front analysis can be understood by everyone without
education.  I have seen english prose about the above and it is too
easy to get tangled into a mess of exceptions to the standard flow
that know one knows what is happening.

Now anyone want to talk about analysis patterns?  This is stuff that
just makes sense out of the box.

http://hillside.net/patterns/patterns.html
	  

Thanks
Ken Foskey
http://www.zipworld.com.au/~waratah/
```

#### ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

- **From:** "DennisHarley" <LegacyBlue@email.msn.com>
- **Date:** 2000-03-04T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ueLgSUgh$GA.259@cpmsnbbsa05>`
- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com>`

```

MEl5133928 <mel5133928@aol.com> wrote in message
news:20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com...
> Group,
>
> What are your views on the difficulty or non-difficulty in making the
> transition from COBOL to say C++ or JAVA? The reason I ask is that I
remember
> seeing an article which quoted a "leading" consultancy :) stating that
COBOL
> programmers performed poorly in this regard.
>
VB, Powerbuilder, Delphi, C++, and Java have all helped to reduce the COBOL
talent pool.

When new technology becomes hot (more money), people are tempted to learn it
seeking a fatter pay check.

People who attempt to learn on their own initiative learn at their own pace,
and are not forced into a frantic effort to learn and apply concurrently.
They have the luxury of failing, and learning from their mistakes. There are
COBOL programmers who know other languages, but don't list them on their
resume.

People who are forced into a new environment are generally not as
interested, and tend to do worse. I have been in shops where new technology
was being introduced. Some people went out and bought book, magazines, etc.
to familiarize themselves with the product. Others sat back and waited until
the employer offered in-house training. If you do your homework, you get
more out of the class. You can ask intelligent questions about the things
you don't understand. People who go in unprepared are usually afraid to ask
questions because they don't want to appear stupid, or ignorant. So they
remain ignorant.

The interest the individual has is probably the most important factor in
whether or not a person is successful.
```

#### ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com>
- **Date:** 2000-03-04T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38C05182.D62293B6@home.com>`
- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com>`

```


MEl5133928 wrote:
> 
> Group,
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Firstly your question is geared "COBOLers switching to C++ or Java". You
didn't mention OO COBOL - a switch to the latter, if you ignore
windowing, dead easy. To effectively use OO COBOL - then some research
on what you are doing to make it really effective.

The problem of switching from COBOL to C++ and Java, is the double
whammy of getting into OO in a strange language. I know nothing of C++
or Java, but let's make a comparison of what of I've read about
Smalltalk, (I haven't programmed in it).

Everything BUT everything in Smalltalk is an object. OO COBOL, as part
of our continuing COBOL standards allows us to use :-

	move pic 9(04)v9(02) to  z,zzz.99 Blank when zero
	move NameA to NameB
	move NameA(3:6) to NameB(7: )

Dead easy in COBOL and no complications. Now take the same Smalltalk
approach and use it with C++ or Java; the COBOL programmer has to
re-invent the wheel learning new syntax. From the little coding I've
seen in either C or Java, these languages gladly let you manipulate bits
and bytes and even allow you to screw-up memory locations with pointers.

Actually Java really pisses off Smalltalk aficionados - and quite
rightly - it is a knock-off of Smalltalk, and so far as I'm aware
doesn't' address one of Smalltalk's best features, which OO COBOl does -
collections and dictionaries.

I feel for those COBOL programmers who get dumped on having to work in
C++ or Java, because these are the new 'neat' languages - and this is
the way to go. I haven't gotten around to looking at it yet in
NetExpress, but when I do I'm willing to bet the mysteries of connecting
to Java, (if it is truly necessary),  can be greatly simplified.

A point worth remembering is that Java and similar languages address a 
technical deficiency - response to inter-active input on the 'Net is
currently just too slow - so you shove it all into one Window, press
OK/SEND and then the host program does a validity check. Now increment
the response time on the 'Net where you can truly have inter-active
input, line by line - the 'mod' languages become irrelevant - and we are
back to the ideal DOS type situation where business logic was controlled
from the Screen Section - not some third party gizmo provided in
something like HTML/Activex.

Given our current rate of technology progress, that increased 'Net speed
I refer to will probably be with us in a 2-5 year time span, or less.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
Just a minor correction - JAVA does not have pointers.  (They are
implied, but never specifically referenced by the programmer).

But to jump on your bandwagon - I'm a JAVA newbee - learning it in my
spare time at work, because we use it within Oracle as a stored
procedure.  I've been doing some other work, on the side, interfacing
to an Object Oriented system - given examples in VB and C++.  One of
my exercises in understanding was to convert a C++ example to COBOL.
I found that the overhead imposed by C++ to do Object Orientation,
especially with Microsoft Foundation Classes is immense.  I was able
to interface with the system with just a few lines of COBOL, as
opposed to 12 header files and numerous modules in C++ - many of them
generated by the MFC class browser.  

The point is, that OO COBOL, having the benefit of coming after some
of the other OO languages, has taken the opportunity to do it "right".


I'll be getting into OO COBOL very shortly.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Cheesle" <cheesle@online.NoSpamPlease.no>
- **Date:** 2000-03-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8a0jfk$cl4$1@news.cerf.net>`
- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com> <38C05182.D62293B6@home.com> <38c1ddbe.138328246@news.cox-internet.com> <38C393B2.1EA2DC20@zip.com.au>`

```
"Ken Foskey" <waratah@zip.com.au> wrote in message
news:38C393B2.1EA2DC20@zip.com.au...
> Delphi is much closer to the true OO path.  There is almost no
> generated code when you use the Delphi screen painter, it is inherited
> as it should be.

Well, there is a file "myform".dfm, which hold a whole lot of information
and your Delphi application wouldn't be worth much without it.

I guess we are confusing the scope a little bit here. Any language need the
initial parameters set, also OO languages like Simula (Delphi is not a
*real* OO language). MFC is a OO wrapper of the Windows low level api.

A very simple definition of the difference of OO and procedural programming
is that while procedural programming has a strict separation of code and
data, the OO implementation show the data and logic as one whole. Consider
for instance a currency calculator. With a procedural approach, we would
have to specify the currency each time we used it. Consider a US company
dealing with various currencies:

    CALL CalcCurrency USING ExchangeRate, Amount GIVING AmountInUSDollar.

If we had multiple currencies we would:
    MOVE value TO Amount
    MOVE 46 TO Currency
    EVALUATE Currency
        When GermanMark
            CALL AmountInUSDollar USING ExchangeRateGermanMark, Amount
                GIVING ItemTotal
                END-CALL
        When NorwegianCrown
        and so on...

If we hade an OO implementation, the Amount itself would know what to do,
by:
(This is pseudo code, NOT OO Cobol!)
    Create Amount as GermanMark Using 46 value
    MOVE Amount.AmountInUSDollar TO ItemTotal

While the obvious benefit does not show here, due to the small undocumented
context, in huge applications it is tremendous, because you do not have to
carry around a lot of parameters, the "data" itself knows what to present
itself.

In contradiction to what has been the major issue in many discussions in
this NG, I for one wouldn't say that it is the UI layer that benefits the
most from OO, but the business logic, because the business logic in many
ways will benefit significantly from abstract methods.

Cheesle
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "DennisHarley" <LegacyBlue@email.msn.com>
- **Date:** 2000-03-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<OMdJON5h$GA.248@cpmsnbbsa05>`
- **References:** `<20000303151450.02510.00000239@ng-cd1.aol.com> <38C05182.D62293B6@home.com> <38c1ddbe.138328246@news.cox-internet.com> <38C393B2.1EA2DC20@zip.com.au> <8a0jfk$cl4$1@news.cerf.net>`

```
Since I work on procedural COBOL systems, I can tell you that there are many
ways to solve the currency conversion problem.

But lets suppose that I am creating a report in a single currency from
transactions in multiple currencies:

1) The transactions would contain the currency code of the amounts in the
transaction. That's the way it is on most systems I have seen.

2) The currency conversion would be handled by called modules. (Not by
currency code, but by type of conversion. i.e. Avg. daily, Avg. monthly.
etc.)

3) The report program would have the currency code used for the report.

The following code might be used:

EVALUATE  TRUE
    WHEN  RPT-CUR-CD  = TRN-CUR-CD
                  MOVE  TRN-AMT  to  RPT-AMT
    WHEN  AVG-DAILY-CUR-CNV-RPT
                 CALL  AVG-DLY-CUR-CNV-PGM
                             USING  RPT-CUR-CD
                                           TRN-CUR-CD
                                           TRN-DT
                                           TRN-AMT
                                           RPT-AMT
    WHEN OTHER
                  CALL  AVG-MTHLY-CUR-CNV-PGM
                              USING  RPT-CUR-CD
                                           TRN-CUR-CD
                                           TRN-DT
                                           TRN-AMT
                                           RPT-AMT
END-EVALUATE.

It isn't as complicated, as your example.

Cheesle <cheesle@online.NoSpamPlease.no> wrote in message
```

```
the possibility of a successful discussion too.

I use both methods, depending on the convenience in the particular task and
I respect both.
```

```
Dennis,
  All the "strategic" IBM mainframe COBOLs already support OO syntax.  That
does NOT mean that shops are actually using it, but it does mean that if your
shop is running a compiler that IBM supports, then it already supports OO.

  If your compiler supports something
       and
  you are doing "new" development
        then
  someone will start using it - and HOPEFULLY showing its usefulness.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** "DennisHarley" <LegacyBlue@email.msn.com>

```
DennisHarley wrote:
> 
> Hopefully the PC environment will spawn OO COBOL talent, which will then
> provide the necessary resources to make OO COBOL on the mainframe more

Forgive me Dennis, and this is not knocking mainframes. But from my days
with mainframes as a systems manager I don't really recall that we ever
got over-excited about our mainframe applications. They were a lot of
sweat and slog to try to satisfy pretty dim and very frustrating users. 

That's not to suggest that there aren't 'interesting' applications on
mainframes, but I'm not aware of any. (Well, that's not quite true - for
our Viking friends, I never made it to Sweden - but as a department
store group we were immensely interested in what Turitz AB had been
doing since about the mid-sixties - for our American brethren they are
the Swedish Woolworth/Walmart).

PC-land can be very frustrating at times matching packages, but I think
for the average PC-programmer it is also very exciting. My bet is unless
it is REAL BIG BUCKS - considerably more than they are earning on PCs -
I just don't see that PC-ers will be the slightest bit interested in
mainframes.  And that suggests that a void could grow up between OO
usage possibly becoming a bright star in PC-land and an oh, so, so,
maybe, with mainframes.

Evidence of this - Howard just can't see where he would ever use/want OO
on a mainframe - I know I'm paraphrasing Howard - but that's how it
comes across.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
"DennisHarley" <LegacyBlue@email.msn.com> wrote in message
news:#XBKwFIi$GA.202@cpmsnbbsa04...
> I am hoping that OOP will come to pass, but from what I have seen this may
> not be the case.

Well, in my opinion, anything has to prove its usability, and thereby the
right to live. As I say, one should use whatever benefits, that's why I
prefer bastards like C++, Delphi, VB and for that matter OO Cobol rather
than pure OO languages as Simula and Smalltalk.

> It is difficult enough to try to get people to use modular programming.

This is a very good point. Lots of people think programming is about
learning a particular language, which is far from the thruth. I have always
said that what counts is to understand programming, then you choose the
language that is appropriate for your task. Which could be C for a device
driver and Cobol for a business application.

This is probably one of the best evidences that programming still is a young
engineering occupation. A friend of mine here in Norway once pointed out
(yes he is a classic engineer) that if there had been allowed as many errors
in traditional engineering as it is in the software business, oil drilling
platforms in the ocean, bridges like the one in San Fransisco and numerous
others, could never have been built.

Traditional engineering is guided by rules and methods, which is yet to come
in software development. Some is there, and used to various extends, but
there is still way to go.

> I think that people who write modular systems will have an easier time,
and
> people who write monolithic programs will find OOP more difficult. The

Sounds reasonable, I second that.

> I would like to blame it on non-technical managers, but I have worked for
some
> non-technicians who were excellent managers.

As many other things in the world, it is all about individual attitude.

Cheesle
```

```
I have seen that line often, but I don't know if I agree with it.  In an

a series of 20 holding a joint together, or a bit of efficiency loss.

single-hulled or that the Romans had lead pipes?  Or should we wait
```

```
I wouldn't disagree with what you are both saying, but we all have our
preferences. In my case I would plunk for collections/dictionaries which
I consider to be a TREMENDOUS feature. Now that's difficult because you
can't judge in the abstract, and at the moment it is only a feature of
NetExpress taken from Smalltalk. It is infinitely flexible and in
designing MyDiaog.cbl to handle ANY dialog, I use a collection to hold
all the windows control objects I throw at MyDialog for any window. I'm
just testing it where I need to allocate dynamically created entryfields
to go into a table. (The quickie solution is to use Activex with a grid
- but I'm proving that with some research, it is easily achievable from
OO COBOL using Dialog Editor/OO GUIs).

Out of curiosity does anybody know if languages other than COBOL 200x
and Smalltalk, allow for collections/dictionaries ?

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
Smalltalk is not related to Simula when it comes to ownership. I suspect the
development of Smalltalk is based on Simula yes. But Simula is definitely a
separate language, I honestly don't know about rights and versions. But I do
know they use Simula at universities here in Norway, So I suspect it is a
live and kicking.

A quick browse on the internet shows it is. It seems like it is registered
with a company (Simula AS) and is managed by the University of Oslo. There
is also a story about it, which you can read here:
    http://www.ifi.uio.no/~cim/sim_history.html
```

```
"James J. Gavan" wrote:
> 
> Out of curiosity does anybody know if languages other than COBOL 200x
> and Smalltalk, allow for collections/dictionaries ?

The C++ standard template libraries contain these.  These are also
staple in any OO application, it is very common to have to iterate
over a collection of objects doing something.  For example if you
expand a window you have to iterate over all the sub windows to get
them to repaint themselves.

Thanks
Ken Foskey
http://www.zipworld.com.au/~waratah/
```

```
Ken Foskey wrote:
> 
> "James J. Gavan" wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Clarification on this one please Ken - are we comparing apples with
apples ? The only reference I have is to the C language 'Win 32
Programming' Rector & Newcomer. An extremely comprehensive book about 
windowing - but try as I might I don't see any reference in the index to
collections/dictionaries. I appreciate C++ does take the language a step
further.

From what you've said, yes any good OO language must have the ability to 
create 'queues'. But what I'm querying is does C++ provide a specific
pre-written tool. What am I asking ? - flat files - OPEN, READ, WRITE,
REWRITE, CLOSE etc. Each COBOL compiler gives us these features - we
don't need to know the internal mechanics but each compiler returns us
the same results. 

Now when it comes to Smalltalk and OO COBOL :-

	invoke SortedCollection "new" returning mySortedCollection
	invoke mySortedCollection "at" returning ls-string
	invoke MyDictionary "includesKey" using ls-key

Pre-canned classes are doing exactly the same as our file verbs. I don't
have to re-invent the wheel, something like say 500 plus methods within
the class/dictionary hierarchy do it for me.

Now when I talk about 'common classes' I don't want to inhibit the
compiler vendors, but whether it is Fujitsu, AcucCOBOL, Liant or Merant
etc. I do want them all to agree on a common terminology (method names)
that have the same meaning/effect as READ, WRITE, REWRITE etc... The
mechanics of how their version of a particular class handles it is
internal, but the common method-names give me the same result from
compiler to compiler. Further, if one particular vendor wants to enhance
collections/dictionaries with additional features - fine.

Now are you saying C++ does this - or just gives you the ability to
write code to do this ?

Jimmy, Calgary AB

> Thanks
> Ken Foskey
> http://www.zipworld.com.au/~waratah/
```

```
"James J. Gavan" wrote:
> 
> Now are you saying C++ does this - or just gives you the ability to
> write code to do this ?

Standard template library (STL) is delivered with all C++ compilers. 
It is public domain source developed about 10 years ago, it does
everything you talk about.

If we are serious about a public domain the C++ STL is the place to
start looking for ideas.  Most programs will use these concepts.

Thanks
Ken Foskey
http://www.zipworld.com.au/~waratah/
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Procedural vs Object-Oriented programming

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** frlsfly@aol.com (FRLSFLY)

```
Interesting that you recommend using the C++ STLs as a model.  My friends who
are currently making a VERY good living by going in to rescue C++  projects in
trouble say that the first thing they do is dump all the (and I quote them
here)  "template crap".  Then the second thing they do is dump all the object
libraries.  Third thing they do is code the applications as close to procedural
'C' as possible, and then cloak that procedural code in C++ object "wrappers"
so their clients can say they are object-oriented.

These are people who have been doing C++ for 10 years or more, and they seem to
know what they speak of.  At least, they all have a LOT more money than I do!

Sigh.


Nathan Meyer
Berkeley, CA
"Big Endian" is what I like.
```
